What Is Kalam?
intermediate
8 min
The Necessity of Ta'alluq Between Knower and Known
Ījī defines ta'alluq as necessary for knowledge and defends its conceptual nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a relation (ta'alluq) between knower and known is necessary for knowledge, and how it is defined and defended by the theologians.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires a relation between knower and known, called ta'alluq.

This passage from al-Mawaqif lays the foundation for understanding knowledge as a relational act. Before we delve into divine knowledge, the author Muḥammad al-Ījī establishes the universally necessary condition: that any act of knowing requires a specific connection between the knower and the known. This connection, technicaly called ta'alluq, is the first objective of the section. Read on to see how he defines it, proves its necessity from innate human experience (fitra), and defends it against a potential misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal translation. Get a sense of the author's style — it's concise and philosophical.
Pay attention to the term 'ta'alluq' which is the central concept.
2
Identify the three parts of the passage
The passage has a clear structure: a definition, a proof from fitra, and an objection-and-answer. Spot each section.
The proof starts with 'فإنَّا إذا أدركنا شيئا...' and the objection with 'فإن قلت...'
3
Understand the proof from fitra
The author argues that when we perceive something, we experience a new state (a relation) that is not due to removal of something but due to a distinction (tamyīz) and appearance. This is intuitively necessary.
This establishes that the relation is directly witnessed by innate disposition.
4
Analyze the objection and its answer
The objection claims ta'alluq only exists between two real things. The answer is that cognitive ta'alluq only requires conceptual multiplicity, not actual existence in the external world or mind.
This clarifies that ta'alluq is a conceptual relation, not necessarily entailing concrete existence.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: why is this distinction important for theology? It allows for a relation between Allah (the knower) and creation (the known) without implying change in Allah.
This will be built upon in later lessons on divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : إضافة ما بين العالم والمعلوم . العلم لا بد فيه من إضافة أي نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم، بها يكون العالم عالماً بذلك المعلوم، والمعلوم معلوماً لذلك العالم، وهو أي ما ذكرناه من الإضافة والنسبة هو الذي نسميه نحن معاشر المتكلمين التعلق، فهذا الأمر المسمى بالتعلق لا بد منه في كون الشيء عالماً بآخر، ولم يثبت غيره بدليل فلذلك اقتصر جمهور المتكلمين عليه. قوله : (العلم لا بد فيه إلخ)، فإنَّا إذا أدركنا شيئاً فلا خفاء في أنه يحصل لنا حال يكاد يشهد الفطرة بأنها بحصول أمر لم يكن لا بزوال أمر كان، وما ذاك إلا بتميز لذلك الشيء وظهوره، فهذا القدر ضروري، وأما ما سواه فأمر يحتاج إلى دليل، وهو الذي يعبر عنه بالإضافة والتعلق. فإن قلت : التعلق إنما يتصور بين الشيئين وذلك في المحققات. قلت : التعلق العلمي يكفيه التعدد والتكثر في المفهومات في أنفسها ولا يستدعي الثبوت في الخارج أو الذهن.
The first objective: The relation between the knower and the known. Knowledge inevitably requires a relation, i.e., a specific connection between the knower and the known, by which the knower is knowing that known thing, and the known is known for that knower. And that which we mentioned of relation and connection is what we, the community of theologians (mutakallimun), call 'ta'alluq' (attachment). So this matter called ta'alluq is necessary for something to be knowing another, and no other [relation] has been proven by evidence, so the majority of theologians restricted themselves to it. His saying: 'Knowledge inevitably requires [a relation], etc.' — For when we perceive something, there is no concealment that a state occurs for us that innate disposition (fitra) almost attests to it being due to the occurrence of something that was not, not due to the removal of something that was. And that is nothing but a distinction (tamyīz) for that thing and its appearance. So this amount is necessary. And as for other than it, it is a matter that requires evidence, and it is that which is expressed by relation and ta'alluq. If you say: Ta'alluq is only conceived between two things, and that is in [?] real things (al-muḥaqqaqāt). I say: Cognitive ta'alluq suffices it is multiplicity and plurality in the concepts themselves, and it does not require existence in the external world or the mind.
Source pages p. 2

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the necessity of a relation (ta'alluq) between the knower and the known for knowledge. It begins by stating the first objective: the relation between knower and known. The author then asserts that knowledge inevitably requires a specific relation (idafa or nisba) by which the knower becomes knowing of a particular known object and that object becomes known to that knower. This relation is technically called 'ta'alluq' by the theologians (mutakallimun). The passage emphasizes that this ta'alluq is indispensable for the act of knowing, and no other relation has been proven by evidence, so the majority of theologians restrict themselves to it. The commentary explains that when we perceive something, a state occurs that innate disposition (fitra) witnesses as being due to a new relation, not the removal of an old one; it is a distinction (tamyīz) and appearance of the thing. This much is necessary and intuitive. Beyond that, any additional aspect requires proof, and that is what is referred to as relation and ta'alluq. A potential objection is raised: ta'alluq is only conceived between two things that are real. The response clarifies that cognitive ta'alluq (ta'alluq 'ilmi) only requires multiplicity and plurality in the concepts themselves, not actual existence in the external world or the mind. This defends the notion of relation as conceptual, not necessarily entailing concrete existence.

Key Terms
إضافة
نسبة
تعلق
عالم
معلوم
فطرة
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ta'alluq requires real existence of both relata in the external world.
Correction: The passage clarifies that cognitive ta'alluq only requires conceptual plurality, not actual existence in the external world or mind.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the majority of theologians restrict themselves to the concept of ta'alluq?
A) Because it is the only relation proven by evidence.
B) Because it is the simplest explanation.
C) Because it is the only one mentioned in scripture.
D) Because it avoids philosophical complexities.

Key Takeaway
Ta'alluq is the necessary relation between knower and known, proven by innate human experience and defended as a conceptual relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something new about your faith, do you sense a personal 'relation' forming between you and that truth? How does this awareness change how seriously you take your studies? Commit to one specific action this week to strengthen that relation.
?
The passage says the relation of knowledge is intuitive and does not require physical proof. Reflect on a time you knew something in your heart to be true despite lacking tangible evidence. What kept that knowledge alive? Will you trust that inner distinction more deeply?
?
The objection suggests that a real connection needs two real things. Are you tempted to dismiss spiritual knowledge because you cannot see it? How can you allow the conceptual reality of ta'alluq to reshape your certainty in unseen truths? What one belief will you actively affirm today based on this lesson?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Knowledge as a Real Attribute with Relation
Definition of knowledge as a real attribute with relation and al-Baqillani's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some Ash'ari theologians define knowledge as a real attribute that possesses a relation, and how al-Baqillani distinguished between the attribute and its relation.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a real attribute, but its 'knowingness' is a relation.

In theological discussions, defining knowledge is crucial. Some Ash'ari scholars propose that knowledge is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) that inherently has a relation (ta'alluq) to what is known. This lesson examines that definition and how al-Bāqillānī carefully distinguished the attribute itself from the resulting relation called al-'ālamiyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms: صفة حقيقية, ذات تعلق, العالمية.
2
Identify the two components
The passage states there are two things: knowledge (the attribute) and al-‘ālamiyya (the relation). Understand the distinction.
The attribute is real; the relation is its effect.
3
Understand al-Bāqillānī's affirmation
The judge al-Bāqillānī affirmed both as distinct. Consider why this distinction is important for divine attributes.
It avoids conflating God's essence with contingent relations.
4
Reflect on the chosen definition
The author says this is the chosen definition. Think about how this definition differs from others in kalam.
Compare with definitions that ignore the relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: هو أي العلم صفة حقيقية ذات تعلق، والقائل به جماعة من الأشاعرة وهم الذين عرفوه بأنه صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض، وقد عرفت أنه المختار من تعريفاته عند المصنف، فلا تغفل. وعلى قول هؤلاء فثمة أمران: هما العلم وهو تلك الصفة والعالمية، أي ذلك التعلق. وأثبت القاضي الباقلاني العلم الذي هو صفة موجودة والعالمية التي هي التعلق.
And it was said: It—i.e., knowledge—is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) possessing a relation (dhāt ta‘alluq). And the proponent of this is a group of the Ash‘arites, who defined it as an attribute that necessitates a distinction (tamyīz) that does not admit of its opposite. And you have come to know that it is the chosen (al-mukhtār) among its definitions according to the author (al-muṣannif), so do not be heedless. And according to the statement of these people, there are two things: knowledge, which is that attribute, and the property of being knowing (al-‘ālamiyya), i.e., that relation. And the judge al-Bāqillānī affirmed knowledge, which is an existing attribute, and al-‘ālamiyya, which is the relation.
Source pages p. 2

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative definition of knowledge and then elaborates on the components of that view. It states that some say knowledge is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) that possesses a relation (ta‘alluq). The proponents are a group of Ash‘arites who define knowledge as an attribute that necessitates a distinction (tamyīz) that does not admit of its opposite (i.e., ignorance). The author notes that this is the chosen definition (al-mukhtār) among the definitions according to the author (al-muṣannif), so the reader should not neglect it. The passage then explains that according to this view, there are two things: (1) knowledge itself, which is that attribute, and (2) al-‘ālamiyya (the property of being knowing), which is the relation (ta‘alluq). The judge al-Bāqillānī is cited as affirming both: knowledge as an existing attribute and al-‘ālamiyya as the relation. This distinguishes between the attribute and the relation, whereas some might conflate them.

Key Terms
صفة حقيقية
ذات تعلق
تمييز
لا يحتمل النقيض
العالمية
القاضي الباقلاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, what are the two things involved in the act of knowing?
A) The knower and the known.
B) The attribute of knowledge and the relation (al-‘ālamiyya).
C) The definition and the proof.
D) The necessary and the contingent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani distinguished the attribute of knowledge itself from its relation (al-‘ālamiyya), affirming both as real but distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God's knowledge is a real attribute with a relation. Does this understanding make you feel that God's knowledge of your deepest secrets is direct and certain? How does that shape your actions when no one is watching?
?
If knowledge in God is an attribute that necessitates distinction without error, how does that contrast with your own knowledge which is prone to mistakes? Are you humble enough to admit your ignorance, or do you cling to your own opinions as if they were certain?
?
Al-Baqillani distinguished the attribute itself from its relation. In your life, do you sometimes focus on the 'attribute' of your knowledge (what you know) while ignoring the 'relation' (how it connects to your actions)? Will you commit to letting your knowledge actually change how you live today?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
The Relation (Ta'alluq) Between Knower and Known
Al-Jurjani defines the necessity of the relation (ta'alluq) between knower and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central role of the relation (ta'alluq) in the mutakallimun's account of knowledge and the differing views on whether knowledge is a qualifier or a relation.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not possible without a relation (ta'alluq) between knower and known.

This lesson introduces the first of sixteen aims in the discussion of knowledge: the relation between knower and known. Al-Jurjani explains that this relation, called ta'alluq, is essential for any act of knowing. He then presents two main positions among the mutakallimun: one that identifies knowledge with this relation, and another that treats knowledge as a qualifier that possesses the relation, leading to different numbers of distinct entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and translation. Notice the technical terms: idafa, nisba, ta'alluq, sifa, `ālimiyya.
The passage contains interlinear glosses in parentheses; read through them.
2
Identify the central claim about ta'alluq
The passage asserts that ta'alluq is necessary for knowledge. Find the sentence that says this.
It is in the first few lines.
3
Trace the different positions
The passage distinguishes the majority mutakallimun, some Ash'arites, and al-Baqillani. For each, list what entities they affirm.
Pay attention to the numbers: 1 entity, 2 entities, 3 or 4.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters for theology. The nature of knowledge relates to divine attributes and how Allah knows.
This lesson sets up a contrast with the philosophers' view in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من الأنواع الخمسة (العلم، وفيه مقاصد ) ستة عشر . المقصد الأول : إضافة ما بين العالم والمعلوم ( العلم لا بد فيه من إضافة أي نسبة مخصوصة ( بين العالم والمعلوم)، بها يكون العالم عالماً بذلك المعلوم، والمعلوم معلوماً لذلك العالم، (وهو) أي ما ذكرناه من الإضافة والنسبة هو الذي نسميه نحن معاشر المتكلمين (التعلق)، فهذا الأمر المسمى بالتعلق لا بد منه في كون الشيء عالماً بآخر، (ولم يثبت غيره بدليل) فلذلك اقتصر جمهور المتكلمين عليه، وقيل: هو ) أي العلم (صفة) حقيقية ذات تعلق) والقائل به جماعة من الأشاعرة وهم الذين عرفوه بأنه صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض، وقد عرفت أنه المختار من تعريفاته عند المصنف، فلا تغفل. وعلى قول هؤلاء فثمة أمران :هما العلم وهو تلك الصفة ( والعالمية)، أي ذلك التعلق ( وأثبت القاضي الباقلاني العلم الذي هو صفة موجودة والعالمية التي هي من قبيل الأحوال عنده، وأثبت معهما تعلقاً فإما للعلم فقط، أو للعالمية فقط، فهاهنا ثلاثة أمور العلم والعالمية والتعلق الثابت لأحدهما ( وأما لهما معاً فهاهنا أربعة أمور العلم والعالمية والتعلق الثابت لهما
The second of the five types (knowledge, and it contains sixteen aims). The first aim: the relation between the knower and the known. (Knowledge necessarily requires an addition, i.e., a specific relation between the knower and the known, through which the knower is knowing of that known and the known is known to that knower. (That is), what we mentioned of addition and relation is what we, the community of mutakallimun, call (ta'alluq). So this entity called ta'alluq is necessary for a thing to be knowing of another. (And no other [entity] has been proven by proof), so the majority of mutakallimun confined themselves to it. And it is said: (knowledge) is a real qualifier (sifa) possessing ta'alluq, and those who say this are a group of Ash'arites, who defined it as a qualifier that necessitates a discrimination that does not admit its opposite. And you have known that it is the chosen of its definitions according to the author, so do not be neglectful. According to the latter, there are two things: knowledge, which is that qualifier, and al-`ālimiyya (knower-ness), i.e., that ta'alluq. And al-Qadi al-Baqillani affirmed knowledge as an existent qualifier and `ālimiyya which, according to him, is of the category of states (ahwal), and he affirmed with them a ta'alluq, either for knowledge alone or for `ālimiyya alone; so here there are three things: knowledge, `ālimiyya, and the ta'alluq affirmed for one of them. (And if for both of them, then here there are four things: knowledge, `ālimiyya, and the ta'alluq affirmed for both).
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of knowledge ('ilm) and the relation (ta'alluq) between the knower and the known according to the mutakallimun. It begins by establishing that the second of the five types discussed in this work is knowledge, which contains sixteen aims; the first aim is the relation between knower and known. The author asserts that knowledge necessarily involves an addition (idafa) or specific relation (nisba) between the knower and the known, through which the knower becomes knowing and the known becomes known. This relation is what the mutakallimun call 'ta'alluq'. The passage emphasizes that this ta'alluq is essential for any act of knowing. Since no other entity besides ta'alluq has been proven as necessary for knowledge, the majority of mutakallimun (jumhur al-mutakallimin) confined themselves to this ta'alluq in their account of knowledge. However, a group of Ash'arites argued that knowledge itself is a real qualifier (sifa haqiqiyya) that possesses ta'alluq; they defined it as a qualifier that entails discrimination (tamyiz) without admitting its opposite. The author notes that this definition is the one chosen by the author (al-Iji) among the various definitions. According to this view, there are two distinct entities: knowledge (the qualifier) and 'ālimiyya (the state of being a knower), which is the ta'alluq itself. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani further refined the position: he affirmed that knowledge is an existent qualifier and that 'ālimiyya is of the category of states (ahwal, a concept in Ash'arite metaphysics). He also affirmed a third entity, a ta'alluq, which could either attach only to knowledge or only to 'ālimiyya, resulting in three total entities. Alternatively, if the ta'alluq attaches to both, then there are four entities: knowledge, 'ālimiyya, and the ta'alluq affirmed for each. This passage sets up the debate about the components of knowledge, which will be contrasted with the philosophers' view in the next lesson.

Key Terms
علم
تعلق
إضافة
نسبة
صفة
عالمية
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all mutakallimun agree that knowledge is the relation (ta'alluq) itself.
Correction: Some Ash'arites consider knowledge to be a qualifier (sifa) that possesses the relation ta'alluq, not identical to it.

Checkpoint
What did the majority of mutakallimun confine themselves to in their account of knowledge, according to the passage?
A) Knowledge is a qualifier (sifa) with ta'alluq
B) Knowledge is the relation (ta'alluq) itself
C) Knowledge is mental existence
D) Knowledge consists of three entities: knowledge, `ālimiyya, and ta'alluq

Key Takeaway
The relation (ta'alluq) is essential for knowledge, but there is dispute over whether knowledge is identical to that relation or a qualifier that possesses it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge requires a real relation (ta'alluq) between knower and known, is your knowledge of Allah merely intellectual information or does it involve a genuine connection that changes how you live? What one evidence from your actions today shows that your knowledge of Allah is truly relational?
?
Consider a piece of knowledge that has deeply affected your behavior (e.g., knowing that a loved one is in danger). How does that compare to your knowledge of Allah’s attributes? Do you feel the same urgency and transformation? If not, what must you do to turn your knowledge into a living ta'alluq?
?
The majority of mutakallimun said that only the relation (ta'alluq) is proven as necessary, nothing else. Are you content with just affirming a connection to Allah without embodying it? What practical step will you take this week to deepen your ta'alluq with your Creator, so that your knowledge becomes a force that reshapes your desires and priorities?

What Is Kalam?

Knowledge as Mental Existence: The Philosophers' Definition
Al-Jurjani states the philosophers' definition and gives an initial argument for mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers define knowledge as mental existence and how they prove it by showing that we can conceive non-existent objects.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the mental existence of forms, not a relation to external reality.

The philosophers (al-ḥukamā') offer a distinctive definition of knowledge as mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). They argue that knowledge consists of the form of the object present in the mind, even when that object has no external reality. In this passage, al-Jurjani presents their view and the initial evidence for it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition
Locate the statement 'العلم هو الوجود الذهني' and note how they equate it with 'حصول الصورة'.
3
Understand the category issue
Notice that the philosophers place knowledge in the category of quality (كيف) but define it by 'acquisition' (حصول), which is not a quality.
This shows they mean mental existence, not acquisition as a process.
4
Recognize the proof from conceiving non-existents
The passage argues that we can conceive impossibilities and geometric shapes, which have no external existence, so knowledge must be mental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحكماء: العلم هو الوجود الذهني أي الموجود الذهني كما قالوا : العلم حصول الصورة، وأرادوا به أنه الصورة الحاصلة على ما صرح به بعضهم ويدل عليه أنهم جعلوا العلم من مقولة الكيف ذلك عرفوه بحصول الصورة، ولا شبهة في أنَّ الحصول ليس من هذه المقولة، وإنما ذهبوا إلى أن العلم هو الوجود الذهني، إذ قد يعقل ما هو نفي محض وعدم صرف في الخارج بحسب الخارج كالممتنعات وكثير من الممكنات كبعض الأشكال الهندسية . ألا ترى أنا نحكم عليها، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بتعقلها ولا شبهة أيضاً في أنَّ بين العاقل والمعقول تعلقاً مخصوصاً كما مر، ( والتعلق، إنما يتصور بين شيئين) متمايزين ولا تمايز إلا بأن يكون لكل منهما ثبوت في الجملة، وإذ لا ثبوت للمعلوم هاهنا في الخارج، فإذاً لا حقيقة له إلا الأمر الموجود في الذهن، وهو أي ذلك الأمر الموجود في الذهن هو (العلم) وأما التعلق المذكور فأمر خارج عن حقيقة العلم لازم لها (و) هو ( المعلوم أيضاً، فإنه باعتبار قيامه بالقوة العاقلة علم، وباعتباره في نفسه من حيث هو معلوم فالعلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات ومختلفان
And the philosophers (al-hukamā') said: knowledge is mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī), i.e., the mentally existent, as they said: knowledge is the acquisition of the form (husūl al-ṣūra). They meant by it that it is the form that has been acquired, as some of them explicitly stated. And it is indicated by the fact that they made knowledge belong to the category of quality (kayf), and they defined it by the acquisition of the form, yet there is no doubt that acquisition (husūl) is not of this category. Rather, they held that knowledge is mental existence, because one may conceive what is pure negation and absolute non-existence in the external world, such as impossibilities and many possibles like some geometric shapes. Do you not see that we pass judgments on them, and that cannot be except by conceiving them? And there is also no doubt that between the intellector (al-‘āqil) and the intellected (al-ma‘qūl) there is a specific relation (ta‘alluq) as previously mentioned. (And the relation is only conceivable between two things) that are distinct, and there is no distinction unless each of them has existence in some sense. Since there is no existence for the intellected here in the external world, it has no reality except as something existent in the mind. That something existent in the mind is (knowledge). As for the aforementioned relation, it is something external to the essence of knowledge, necessary for it. (And) it is also (the intellected), for by virtue of its inherence in the intellective faculty it is knowledge, and by virtue of itself insofar as it is intellected, it is the intellected. So knowledge and the intellected are identical in essence and different in consideration.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (al-hukamā') definition of knowledge as mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). The philosophers are identified as those who define knowledge as 'the acquisition of the form' (ḥusūl al-ṣūra), meaning the mental form that represents the object known. Although they placed knowledge in the category of quality (kayf) and defined it by acquisition, the author notes that 'acquisition' itself is not a quality, so their actual position is that knowledge is mental existence. The argument proceeds as follows: we can conceive things that have no external existence—like impossibilities (mumtani‘āt) or possible geometric shapes. We can judge them, which requires conceiving them. Now, between the intellector (al-‘āqil) and the intellected (al-ma‘qūl) there is a relation (ta‘alluq), but a relation requires two distinct things with some sort of existence. Since the intellected may have no external existence, it must have existence in the mind. Therefore, that mental existence is what we call knowledge. The relation itself is not knowledge; it is an accident necessary for knowledge. Furthermore, the intellected, in itself, is the same mental existence: by its inherence in the intellective faculty it is called knowledge, and by itself taken as the object of intellection it is called the intellected. Thus, knowledge and the intellected are identical in essence (muttaḥidān bi al-dhāt) but differ in aspect (mukhtalifān bi al-i‘tibār).

Key Terms
حكماء
الوجود الذهني
حصول الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can we conceive things that have no external existence?
A) Because those things have some external existence
B) Because knowledge is a relation between two existents
C) Because knowledge is mental existence that represents them
D) Because the intellect creates those things in the external world

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define knowledge as mental existence, arguing that we can conceive objects without external reality, so knowledge must be an internal mental form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Before this lesson, did you assume your knowledge of God, angels, and the unseen was identical to their true reality? How does this definition challenge you to scrutinize the mental forms you hold about the unseen? What will you do to ensure your mental representations align with revelation?
?
Think of a belief you hold with absolute certainty—like your belief in the Prophet ﷺ. According to the passage, even that certainty is a mental existence. Does this make you uncomfortable? Does it make your conviction feel less real? If so, what does that reveal about your reliance on external proof rather than internal conviction?
?
If knowledge is mental existence, then your entire understanding of the world is internal. If you discovered tomorrow that a core belief you hold—say, about the nature of the afterlife—was actually a mental construct that did not match reality, would you be willing to abandon it? How does this possibility motivate you to study the Kalam arguments more deeply?

What Is Kalam?

The Identity of Knowledge, Knower, and Known
Al-Jurjani completes the argument by discussing the relation and the identity of knowledge and the intellected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers conclude that knowledge and the intellected are identical in essence but differ in aspect.

Core Idea
Knowledge and the known are the same mental existence viewed from different aspects.

Al-Jurjani continues his presentation of the philosophers' theory by addressing the relation between the knower and the known. He argues that since the known may have no external existence, the relation cannot be between two external things; rather, the mental existence of the known is itself knowledge. The relation is a necessary accident, not the essence of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the second half of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the flow of argument.
2
Understand the argument from relation (ta'alluq)
The passage states that a relation requires two distinct existents. Since the known may not exist externally, it must exist mentally.
3
Distinguish knowledge from the relation
The relation (ta'alluq) is a necessary accident, not the essence of knowledge. Knowledge is the mental existence itself.
4
Grasp the identity of knowledge and the intellected
The same mental existence is called 'knowledge' when considered as inhering in the intellect, and 'the intellected' when considered as the object of intellection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحكماء: العلم هو الوجود الذهني أي الموجود الذهني كما قالوا : العلم حصول الصورة، وأرادوا به أنه الصورة الحاصلة على ما صرح به بعضهم ويدل عليه أنهم جعلوا العلم من مقولة الكيف ذلك عرفوه بحصول الصورة، ولا شبهة في أنَّ الحصول ليس من هذه المقولة، وإنما ذهبوا إلى أن العلم هو الوجود الذهني، إذ قد يعقل ما هو نفي محض وعدم صرف في الخارج بحسب الخارج كالممتنعات وكثير من الممكنات كبعض الأشكال الهندسية . ألا ترى أنا نحكم عليها، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بتعقلها ولا شبهة أيضاً في أنَّ بين العاقل والمعقول تعلقاً مخصوصاً كما مر، ( والتعلق، إنما يتصور بين شيئين) متمايزين ولا تمايز إلا بأن يكون لكل منهما ثبوت في الجملة، وإذ لا ثبوت للمعلوم هاهنا في الخارج، فإذاً لا حقيقة له إلا الأمر الموجود في الذهن، وهو أي ذلك الأمر الموجود في الذهن هو (العلم) وأما التعلق المذكور فأمر خارج عن حقيقة العلم لازم لها (و) هو ( المعلوم أيضاً، فإنه باعتبار قيامه بالقوة العاقلة علم، وباعتباره في نفسه من حيث هو معلوم فالعلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات ومختلفان
And the philosophers (al-hukamā') said: knowledge is mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī), i.e., the mentally existent, as they said: knowledge is the acquisition of the form (husūl al-ṣūra). They meant by it that it is the form that has been acquired, as some of them explicitly stated. And it is indicated by the fact that they made knowledge belong to the category of quality (kayf), and they defined it by the acquisition of the form, yet there is no doubt that acquisition (husūl) is not of this category. Rather, they held that knowledge is mental existence, because one may conceive what is pure negation and absolute non-existence in the external world, such as impossibilities and many possibles like some geometric shapes. Do you not see that we pass judgments on them, and that cannot be except by conceiving them? And there is also no doubt that between the intellector (al-‘āqil) and the intellected (al-ma‘qūl) there is a specific relation (ta‘alluq) as previously mentioned. (And the relation is only conceivable between two things) that are distinct, and there is no distinction unless each of them has existence in some sense. Since there is no existence for the intellected here in the external world, it has no reality except as something existent in the mind. That something existent in the mind is (knowledge). As for the aforementioned relation, it is something external to the essence of knowledge, necessary for it. (And) it is also (the intellected), for by virtue of its inherence in the intellective faculty it is knowledge, and by virtue of itself insofar as it is intellected, it is the intellected. So knowledge and the intellected are identical in essence and different in consideration.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (al-hukamā') definition of knowledge as mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). The philosophers are identified as those who define knowledge as 'the acquisition of the form' (ḥusūl al-ṣūra), meaning the mental form that represents the object known. Although they placed knowledge in the category of quality (kayf) and defined it by acquisition, the author notes that 'acquisition' itself is not a quality, so their actual position is that knowledge is mental existence. The argument proceeds as follows: we can conceive things that have no external existence—like impossibilities (mumtani‘āt) or possible geometric shapes. We can judge them, which requires conceiving them. Now, between the intellector (al-‘āqil) and the intellected (al-ma‘qūl) there is a relation (ta‘alluq), but a relation requires two distinct things with some sort of existence. Since the intellected may have no external existence, it must have existence in the mind. Therefore, that mental existence is what we call knowledge. The relation itself is not knowledge; it is an accident necessary for knowledge. Furthermore, the intellected, in itself, is the same mental existence: by its inherence in the intellective faculty it is called knowledge, and by itself taken as the object of intellection it is called the intellected. Thus, knowledge and the intellected are identical in essence (muttaḥidān bi al-dhāt) but differ in aspect (mukhtalifān bi al-i‘tibār).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
For the philosophers, knowledge and the known are identical in essence, differing only in aspect: the same mental existence is knowledge when considered as inhering in the intellect and the intellected when considered as the object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to 'know' things—your beliefs, your convictions, even your faith. But if, as al-Jurjani argues, the known object in your mind is identical to the knowledge itself, then what does that say about the certainty you feel? Can you distinguish between the knowledge and the thing known, or are they the same mental reality? Examine one strong conviction you hold: is your certainty a separate thing from the knowledge, or is it the knowledge itself? How does this collapse the distance between you and what you claim to know?
?
Al-Jurjani says the relation between knower and known is a necessary accident of knowledge—not separate from it. Think of a time you were absolutely certain of a truth (e.g., that God exists, that the Prophet is truthful). Was that certainty a relation between you and that truth, or was it the very substance of your knowledge? If the relation is not separate, then your knowledge is not just a connection to reality—it is reality itself in your mind. Does that make your knowledge more real or more subjective? What does this imply about how you treat your own intellectual convictions?
?
If the intellected has no existence outside the mind, then the objects of your deepest reflections—God, the afterlife, justice—exist mentally but are identical to your knowledge of them. Does this make them less real, or does it elevate the status of your mind? When you think about God, is the God in your mind the same as your knowledge of Him? How does this affect the way you approach theology and worship? Commit to one change in how you think about your knowledge of divine matters after reading this passage.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Knowledge as Mental Existence and the Identity of Knower and Known
Defines knowledge as mental existence and argues for identity of knower and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers identify knowledge with mental existence and how this leads to the identity of knowledge and the known in essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is mental existence; the knower and known are identical in essence.

This lesson dives into a profound claim from the philosophers: that knowledge is not a relation but mental existence itself. By examining how we conceive non-existent things, we see that the known must exist in the mind. This leads to the surprising conclusion that knowledge and the known are one in essence, differing only in how we consider them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts by referencing a previous discussion on states (ahwal).
2
Identify the philosophers' definition
Find where the passage states that knowledge is mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). Notice how they equate it with 'attainment of the form' (husul al-sura).
3
Follow the argument from conceiving non-existents
The passage gives an argument: we conceive of impossibles and geometric shapes that have no external existence. So they must have mental existence. This shows that knowledge cannot be simply a relation to an external object.
This is a key proof for mental existence.
4
Understand the relation and identity
The passage argues that a relation requires two distinct things. Since the known has no external existence, it must be identical with the mental existence which is knowledge. Thus knowledge and known are united in essence, differing only in consideration.
This leads to the identity thesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومع النوع الثاني - المقصد الأول : إضافة ما بين العالم والمعلوم هي من قبيل الأحوال عنده، وأثبت ) معهما تعلقاً فإما للعلم فقط، أو للعالمية فقط، فهاهنا ثلاثة أمور العلم والعالمية والتعلق الثابت لأحدهما ( وأما لهما معاً فهاهنا أربعة أمور العلم والعالمية والتعلق الثابت لهما ، ( وقال الحكماء: العلم هو الوجود الذهني ) أي الموجود الذهني كما قالوا : العلم حصول الصورة، وأرادوا به أنه الصورة الحاصلة على ما صرح به بعضهم ويدل عليه أنهم جعلوا العلم من مقولة الكيف ذلك عرفوه بحصول الصورة، ولا شبهة في أنَّ الحصول ليس من هذه المقولة، وإنما ذهبوا إلى أن العلم هو الوجود الذهني، إذ قد يعقل ما هو نفي محض وعدم صرف في الخارج بحسب الخارج كالممتنعات وكثير من الممكنات كبعض الأشكال الهندسية . ألا ترى أنا نحكم عليها، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بتعقلها ولا شبهة أيضاً في أنَّ بين العاقل والمعقول تعلقاً مخصوصاً كما مر، ( والتعلق، إنما يتصور بين شيئين) متمايزين ولا تمايز إلا بأن يكون لكل منهما ثبوت في الجملة، وإذ لا ثبوت للمعلوم هاهنا في الخارج، فإذاً لا حقيقة له إلا الأمر الموجود في الذهن، وهو أي ذلك الأمر الموجود في الذهن هو (العلم) وأما التعلق المذكور فأمر خارج عن حقيقة العلم لازم لها (و) هو ( المعلوم أيضاً، فإنه باعتبار قيامه بالقوة العاقلة علم، وباعتباره في نفسه من حيث هو معلوم فالعلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات ومختلفان
And with the second type - first goal: the relation between the knower and the known. It is of the kind of states (ahwal) according to him. And he affirmed with them a relation: either for knowledge only, or for knowership only, so here three things: knowledge, knowership, and the relation affirmed for one of them. And if for both together, then here four things: knowledge, knowership, and the relation affirmed for both. And the philosophers said: knowledge is mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), i.e., the mental existent, as they said: knowledge is the attainment of the form (husul al-sura). And they meant by it that it is the attained form, as some of them explicitly stated, and it indicates that they made knowledge of the category of quality (kayf). They defined it by attainment of form, and there is no doubt that attainment is not of this category. They only held that knowledge is mental existence, since one can conceive what is pure negation and sheer non-existence in the exterior, like impossibles and many possibles such as some geometric shapes. Do you not see that we judge about them, and that is only possible by conceiving them. And there is also no doubt that between the intellect and the intelligible there is a particular relation as mentioned. And the relation is only conceivable between two things that are distinct, and there is no distinction unless each has some sort of affirmation. Since there is no affirmation of the known here in the exterior, then it has no reality except the thing existing in the mind, and that thing existing in the mind is knowledge. And the mentioned relation is something external to the reality of knowledge, necessary for it, and it is also the known. For considered as subsisting in the intellective faculty, it is knowledge, and considered in itself as it is known, knowledge and the known are united in essence and different.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by outlining the section on the relation between knower and known. It explains that according to the author (likely al-Iji), the relation between knowledge and the known is of the kind of states (ahwal). Then it states the position of the philosophers (al-hukama') that knowledge is mental existence. The passage defends this by noting that we conceive of non-existent things, so their reality must be in the mind. Since relation requires two distinct things, and the known has no external existence, it must exist in the mind. Thus knowledge is that mental entity, and the relation is extrinsic. Consequently, knowledge and known are identical in essence but differ in consideration.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
العلم
المعلوم
التعلق
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge is a relation between a mind and an external object, and that the known exists independently in the external world.
Correction: The passage argues that the relation is only between the intellect and the mental entity. The known has no external existence; it is identical with the mental existence that is knowledge. Thus knowledge is not a relation but the mental entity itself.

Checkpoint

B) It is mental existence
C) It is the form in the external world
D) It is a negation

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is mental existence; the known exists in the mind, so knowledge and known are identical in essence, differing only in consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If that mental entity is identical to the known, then what does that say about the reality of your faith? Are you satisfied that your mental conception of Allah is true knowledge, or do you hunger for a direct vision? How can you deepen that mental existence into something that transforms your heart?
?
You recite the Shahada daily: 'There is no god but Allah.' That statement exists as a mental entity in your mind. According to this lesson, that mental existence is knowledge itself. Is your knowledge of the Shahada merely a concept, or does it carry the weight of certainty that moves your limbs? What would it take for that mental existence to become a lived reality that drives every action?
?
This passage argues that the known does not need external existence to be known. Consider your own death—it has not yet happened externally, yet you can conceive it. Is that mental existence enough to prepare you for it? Or do you treat it as an abstract idea, distant and unreal? If the mental existence is real and identical to your knowledge, then how real is your death to you right now? What will you do today to make that mental reality more present in your life?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
First Proof: Coexistence of Opposites
First argument from mutual exclusion of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's first argument against the view that knowledge is mental existence of the known essence, and why it leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
If knowledge were presence of essences, thinking opposites would make the mind contain both.

The mutakallimun offer two reasons why knowledge cannot be the mental existence of the known form. The first argument targets the impossibility of opposite essences coexisting in the mind. Let's examine this logical objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument once, then its translation. Note the structure: premise, application, absurd consequence.
The argument is concise; pay attention to the conditional structure.
2
Identify the premise
Find the condition: if intellection is the obtaining of the intelligible's essence in the mind.
This is the view the mutakallimun are rejecting.
3
Follow the consequence
The arguer applies the premise to the case of thinking of blackness and whiteness. Understand why this leads to the mind being both black and white.
This is the absurdity.
4
Recognize the additional absurdity
Besides the mind having opposed attributes, opposites would coexist in one locus, which is impossible.
This strengthens the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المتكلمون : هو ( أي كون العلم عبارة عن الوجود الذهني ) باطل لوجهين : الأول : لو كان التعقل بحصول ماهية المعقول ) في ذهن العاقل ( فمن عقل السواد والبياض ) وحكم بتضادهما ( يكون قد حصل في ذهنه السواد والبياض فيكون الذهن أسود وأبيض)، إذ لا معنى للأسود والأبيض إلا ما حصل فيه ماهية السواد والبياض، لكنه باطل قطعاً، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية عنه، ( وأيضاً يجتمع الضدان ) في محل واحد وهو سفسطة الوجه ( الثاني : حصول ماهية الجبل والسماء في ذهننا معلوم الانتفاء بالضرورة ) وتجويزه وجود لها أصلاً
And the mutakallimun said: It (i.e., that knowledge is mental existence) is false for two reasons: First: If intellection occurs by the essence of the intelligible obtaining in the mind of the intellector, then whoever intellects blackness and whiteness and judges their mutual opposition would have blackness and whiteness obtaining in his mind, so the mind would be black and white. For there is no meaning of 'black' and 'white' except that in which the essence of blackness and whiteness obtains. But this is absolutely false, because these attributes are negated of it. Also, two opposites would coexist in a single locus, which is sophistry. Second: The obtaining of the essence of a mountain and the sky in our mind is known by necessity to be impossible, and its affirmation is absolutely impossible.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments used by the mutakallimun to refute the theory that knowledge consists in the mental existence of the known form. The first argument: If intellection were the presence of the essence of the thing in the mind, then when someone thinks of black and white, both essences would be present in the mind, making the mind both black and white, which is absurd. Moreover, opposites would coexist in the same locus, which is impossible. The second argument: It is self-evident that the essence of a large object like a mountain or the sky cannot fit into the small space of the mind. Thus, the theory is false. The commentator later explains that if the mental existence is merely an image, it is not the same as the actual object, but the mutakallimun here are arguing that the theory posits the very essence, which leads to these absurdities.

Key Terms
تعقل
معقول
العلم
الوجود الذهني
ضدان
السواد والبياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun reject any form of mental existence.
Correction: They only reject that knowledge is the presence of the actual essence; they accept mental images or concepts in another sense.

Checkpoint
What is the first absurdity the mutakallimun derive from the theory that knowledge is mental existence of essences?
A) The mind cannot hold large essences.
B) The mind would become black and white when thinking of both.
C) Knowledge would be impossible.
D) The mind would be infinite.

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that the mental existence theory would require opposite essences to coexist in the mind, leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself holding two contradictory beliefs about your own character—like thinking you are both generous and stingy at the same time? How does this lesson's argument reveal that such contradictions cannot literally be 'inside' you as essences, and what does that mean for how you examine your own heart?
?
The mutakallimūn used logical arguments to defend the oneness of Allah. In what area of your life do you most need to align your thinking with tawḥīd, even when your desires pull you in opposite directions? Will you commit to one specific change today?
?
If knowledge is not the presence of objects in the mind, then what is the relationship between your thoughts and reality? Does this lesson challenge you to treat your mental images of Allah’s attributes as mere shadows rather than the truth? How would that change your worship?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
3 min
Second Proof: Large Essences Cannot Fit
Second argument from impossibility of large objects' essences in mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's second argument against knowledge as mental existence: the impossibility of large physical essences, like a mountain, fitting into the mind.

Core Idea
If knowledge were mental existence, mountains and skies would have to fit in your head.

After rejecting the first argument, the mutakallimun offer a second, even more direct argument: it is self-evident that large objects cannot have their essences present in the small space of the mind. Let's see how this refutes the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic for the second argument and its translation. Notice how it appeals to necessary knowledge.
This argument is even briefer than the first.
2
Understand the premise
The argument says that the obtaining of the essence of a mountain or sky in the mind is known by necessity to be impossible.
It relies on a self-evident truth.
3
See the conclusion
If the theory claimed that knowledge requires the actual essence in the mind, then such knowledge would be impossible, but we do have knowledge of mountains and skies.
The theory is thus false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المتكلمون : هو ( أي كون العلم عبارة عن الوجود الذهني ) باطل لوجهين : الأول : لو كان التعقل بحصول ماهية المعقول ) في ذهن العاقل ( فمن عقل السواد والبياض ) وحكم بتضادهما ( يكون قد حصل في ذهنه السواد والبياض فيكون الذهن أسود وأبيض)، إذ لا معنى للأسود والأبيض إلا ما حصل فيه ماهية السواد والبياض، لكنه باطل قطعاً، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية عنه، ( وأيضاً يجتمع الضدان ) في محل واحد وهو سفسطة الوجه ( الثاني : حصول ماهية الجبل والسماء في ذهننا معلوم الانتفاء بالضرورة ) وتجويزه وجود لها أصلاً
And the mutakallimun said: It (i.e., that knowledge is mental existence) is false for two reasons: First: If intellection occurs by the essence of the intelligible obtaining in the mind of the intellector, then whoever intellects blackness and whiteness and judges their mutual opposition would have blackness and whiteness obtaining in his mind, so the mind would be black and white. For there is no meaning of 'black' and 'white' except that in which the essence of blackness and whiteness obtains. But this is absolutely false, because these attributes are negated of it. Also, two opposites would coexist in a single locus, which is sophistry. Second: The obtaining of the essence of a mountain and the sky in our mind is known by necessity to be impossible, and its affirmation is absolutely impossible.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments used by the mutakallimun to refute the theory that knowledge consists in the mental existence of the known form. The first argument: If intellection were the presence of the essence of the thing in the mind, then when someone thinks of black and white, both essences would be present in the mind, making the mind both black and white, which is absurd. Moreover, opposites would coexist in the same locus, which is impossible. The second argument: It is self-evident that the essence of a large object like a mountain or the sky cannot fit into the small space of the mind. Thus, the theory is false. The commentator later explains that if the mental existence is merely an image, it is not the same as the actual object, but the mutakallimun here are arguing that the theory posits the very essence, which leads to these absurdities.

Key Terms
العلم
الوجود الذهني
الجبل
السماء
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the second argument?
A) Logical impossibility of opposites.
B) Self-evident impossibility of large essences fitting in the mind.
C) The mind is not a physical place.
D) The mountain is too big to be known.

Key Takeaway
The second argument relies on our intuitive knowledge that physical essences, such as mountains or skies, cannot physically exist in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the essence of a mountain cannot fit in your mind, what does that say about your attempts to fully understand the Creator of that mountain? Do you still think you can claim to have 'grasped' Allah with your intellect, or does this humble you before His majesty?
?
This argument shows that knowledge is not about possessing the essence of things in your mind. When you learn about the Day of Judgment, do you treat that knowledge as a mere mental concept, or does it shake you to your core and change how you live today?
?
The mutakallimun argued that if knowledge were mental existence, we would have mountains in our heads. Have you ever reduced your faith to 'mental acceptance' of doctrines, without allowing them to transform your heart and actions? What would it take for you to move from mental possession to sincere submission?

Answer to the First Objection: The Mind is Not Physically Colored
Distinguishes mental and external existence to refute the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowing blackness does not make the mind black, based on the distinction between mental and external existence.

Core Idea
Mental existence carries only the form, not the physical attributes.

The first objection claims that if external things exist in the mind, the mind would become black when knowing blackness. The commentator shows this confuses the quiddity existing in the mind (a mere intellectual form) with the quiddity existing externally (which carries physical accidents). This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the commentator defines mental existence as 'shadow existence' and external existence as 'original'.
2
Identify the two modes of existence
Find where the commentator distinguishes between the quiddity existing externally and mentally. Note the terms: الوجود الخارجي and الوجود الذهني.
3
Understand why the objection fails
The commentator argues that opposition and color are properties of external existence, so the mind cannot be colored. Reflect on why the objection would only apply if the identity were external.
4
Connect to larger kalam epistemology
This distinction is foundational in Islamic epistemology, showing that knowledge is the presence of a conceptual form, not a physical transfer.

الحاصلة. قوله : ( وجواب الوجه الثاني إلخ) خلاصة الجوابين الفرق بين الوجودين الخارجي والظلي بأن يترتب الآثار من التضاد والعظم والصغر والاتصاف بما هو مسلوب عن الذهن مخصوص بالوجود الخارجي. وما قيل : إِنَّ هذا الجواب لا يجري لو أورد الإشكال بلوازم الماهية، فمدفوع بأن المراد بالوجود الخارجي الأصيلي، ولا شك أن لوازم الماهية لها وجود أصيلي تترتب عليه الآثار، ووجود ظلي فيجري الفرق بين الوجودين فيها أيضاً . هذا منقوض بالمرآة فإنه ينطبع فيها قريب من نصف كرة العالم فلا يسمع دعوى الضرورة في بطلانه، ويجاب بمنع انطباع المرئي في المرآة بل الرؤية بها بطريق الانعكاس والدليل عليه أن من رأى صورة شيء واقع في مقابلة المرآة في موضع معين منها، ثم انتقل الرائي من مكانه إلى مكان آخر من غير انتقال من المرآة وما وقع في مقابلتها يرى تلك الصورة في موضع آخر من المرآة ولو كانت الصورة منطبعة لاستحال ذلك . قوله : ( أن الماهية تطلق بالاشتراك على معنيين.

والسماء) في ذهننا، فإنَّ هذه الهوية هي المتصفة بالعظم المانع من الحصول في أذهاننا، لا ماهيتهما) إذ ليس فيها ما يمنع من حصولها فيها، (وهذا) الذي ذكره المتكلمون في هاتين الشبهتين ( غلط واقع من جهة اشتراك اللفظ فإنَّ الماهية) أي لفظها ( تطلق على الأمر المعقول الذي هو الماهية الموجودة بالوجود الذهني) وعلى ما يطابقه) أي يطابق ذلك الأمر المعقول وهو الموجود الخارجي ( فظن أمرا واحداً، وبنى عليه اشتراكهما في الأحكام ،كلها وقد تبين لك فساد ذلك الظن.
Pure obstinacy. (Answer to the first objection:) It would only necessitate the mind being white and black if the identity of blackness and whiteness occurred in it—i.e., their quiddity existing by external existence called external existence, which is the source of external effects and manifestation of judgments—not their quiddity existing by shadow existence called mental existence. For you have learned in the discussions of mental existence that there is no meaning for quiddity except the intellectual form characterized by non-original existence. And you also learned that it (the intellectual form) differs from external identities characterized by original existences in the concomitants that pertain to the external existent by virtue of a particular role in them, as you have noted before. The location being black and white, as well as opposition, are of the type in which external existence has a role, so it does not necessitate the mind being characterized by what is definitely denied of it, nor the conjunction of two opposites. (And) the answer to the second objection: that it is impossible for the identity of the mountain to occur in a specific thing that has a specific proportionality such that it becomes a mirror for observing it—rather, it is like taking the form of an elephant in a grain of copper. I said: In this there is an admission that what occurs is not the quiddity of the mountain, and that is what is required. And by this the answer becomes clear to what was said: that the image of the mountain and the sky is impressed in the mirror despite its smallness—only that impression in it is denied. His saying (peace be upon him): (And the answer to the first, etc.) And it may be answered by the distinction between obtaining in the mind and obtaining in the locus: the first is circumstantial and the second is attributive. But that is nothing because the obtaining of the form in the mind necessitates the attribute of being knowing. Indeed, that would be complete if knowledge were other than the obtained form. His saying: (And the answer to the second objection, etc.) The summary of the two answers is the distinction between the two existences, external and shadowy: that the effects of opposition, greatness, and smallness, and the attribute of what is denied of the mind, are specific to external existence. And what is said: that this answer does not apply if the problem is brought concerning the concomitants of quiddity—it is repelled because by external existence is meant the original, and there is no doubt that the concomitants of quiddity have an original existence on which the effects follow, and a shadow existence, so the distinction between the two existences applies in them as well. This is refuted by the mirror, for it imprints nearly half the sphere of the universe, so the claim of necessity in its falsity is not heard. And it is answered by denying the impression of the visible in the mirror; rather, seeing by it is by way of reflection. The proof is that one who sees the image of something in front of the mirror at a specific spot, then moves from his place to another place without the mirror or what faces it moving, sees that image in another spot of the mirror. If the image were impressed, that would be impossible. His saying: (And the answer to the first objection, etc.) You have already known in the discussion of mental existence what is in this answer, so refer to it. His saying: (And there is no meaning for quiddity except the intellectual form) i.e., there is no meaning for it in this context except that, so this exclusivity does not conflict with what will come that quiddity is used by homonymy for two meanings. (Page 10) and the sky) in our mind: for this identity is characterized by greatness preventing its occurrence in our minds, not their quiddity, for there is nothing in it to prevent its occurrence in them. (And this) which the theologians mentioned in these two ambiguities (is an error arising from homonymy, for quiddity) i.e., its word (is applied to the intelligible thing which is the quiddity existing by mental existence) and to what corresponds to it) i.e., corresponds to that intelligible thing, which is the external existent (so they supposed it one thing and built upon it their sharing in all judgments. And you have seen the falsity of that supposition.)
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an answer to two objections raised by theologians against the theory of mental existence. The first objection argues that if external things existed in the mind, the mind would become black when knowing blackness and white when knowing whiteness, leading to impossible contradictions. The second objection argues that large things like a mountain and sky cannot fit into the small mind. The commentator, following the tradition of the scholastic theologians, responds by distinguishing between external existence (al-wujud al-khariji) and mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). External existence carries physical attributes and effects—like color, size, and opposition—whereas mental existence is only the conceptual form (al-sura al-‘aqliyya) that represents the thing without its physical concomitants. Thus, the mind is not actually colored or filled; it merely has the intellectual form. The commentator then addresses a clever objection about mirrors: if a mirror can reflect large scenes, why cannot the mind? He denies that images are literally impressed in mirrors, arguing that seeing occurs by reflection, which can shift with perspective, proving it is not a real impression. Finally, he exposes the root error: the theologians equivocated on the term 'quiddity' (mahiyya), applying it both to the mental concept and to the external reality, then falsely assumed they are identical and share all attributes. The lesson thus reinforces the foundational distinction in Muslim epistemology between the two modes of existence.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الماهية
الصورة العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental existence requires the mind to become physically like the object known.
Correction: Mental existence is only the conceptual form, carrying no physical attributes. The mind does not become black when knowing blackness.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what kind of existence does the quiddity that occurs in the mind have?
A) External existence
B) Mental existence
C) Both external and mental
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The mind receives only the intellectual form of a thing, not its physical properties, so knowing blackness does not make the mind black.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that when you think about a difficult or painful situation, it 'enters' you and changes you. But this lesson teaches that only the intellectual form, not the actual burden, enters your mind. How might you live differently today knowing that the weight of your thoughts is not the thing itself? What specific thought have you been carrying that you can now see as just a shadow, not the external reality? Write down one action you will take to release that shadow.
?
When you judge someone negatively, do you feel as though they become 'black' in your mind? This lesson says the mind is not physically colored by its objects, but our emotional reactions often confuse the mental form with the real thing. Can you identify one person you have judged harshly? How can you consciously separate the mental image you have of them from their actual external identity? Commit to one act of fairness toward them today.
?
The objector’s mistake was equating mental existence with external existence in terms of attributes. In your own life, where do you mistakenly treat your thoughts about something as if they were the thing itself—for example, worrying about a future event as if it has already happened? This lesson gives you a tool to distinguish. What is one worry you can now reframe as merely an intellectual form, not a reality? Describe a concrete step to combat that worry.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

Answer to the Second Objection: Large Objects in a Small Mind
Refutes the size objection and explains mirror analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends mental existence against the objection that large objects cannot fit into the mind, using the distinction between external and mental existence and a discussion of mirror images.

Core Idea
Size is a property of external existence; mental forms have no spatial dimensions.

The second objection argues that large external objects like a mountain and sky cannot fit into the small mind. The commentator responds by again distinguishing the two existences and then addresses a clever counterexample using mirrors, showing that even in mirrors images are not literally impressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how the commentator handles the mountain and sky example.
2
Understand the core reply
The commentator says what is impossible is the external identity of the mountain with its specific proportionality occurring in the mind. The mind only has the intellectual form.
3
Examine the mirror objection
Some object that if a mirror can reflect something large, why can't the mind? The commentator denies that impression occurs in mirrors; vision is by reflection.
4
Reflect on the proof from perspective shift
The commentator gives a proof: if you move, the image shifts, which would not happen if it were impressed. This shows the mirror does not contain the actual object.

If the image were impressed, that would be impossible. His saying: (And the answer to the first objection, etc.) You have already known in the discussion of mental existence what is in this answer, so refer to it. His saying: (And there is no meaning for quiddity except the intellectual form) i.e., there is no meaning for it in this context except that, so this exclusivity does not conflict with what will come that quiddity is used by homonymy for two meanings. (Page 10) and the sky) in our mind: for this identity is characterized by greatness preventing its occurrence in our minds, not their quiddity, for there is nothing in it to prevent its occurrence in them. (And this) which the theologians mentioned in these two ambiguities (is an error arising from homonymy, for quiddity) i.e., its word (is applied to the intelligible thing which is the quiddity existing by mental existence) and to what corresponds to it) i.e., corresponds to that intelligible thing, which is the external existent (so they supposed it one thing and built upon it their sharing in all judgments. And you have seen the falsity of that supposition.)
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an answer to two objections raised by theologians against the theory of mental existence. The first objection argues that if external things existed in the mind, the mind would become black when knowing blackness and white when knowing whiteness, leading to impossible contradictions. The second objection argues that large things like a mountain and sky cannot fit into the small mind. The commentator, following the tradition of the scholastic theologians, responds by distinguishing between external existence (al-wujud al-khariji) and mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). External existence carries physical attributes and effects—like color, size, and opposition—whereas mental existence is only the conceptual form (al-sura al-‘aqliyya) that represents the thing without its physical concomitants. Thus, the mind is not actually colored or filled; it merely has the intellectual form. The commentator then addresses a clever objection about mirrors: if a mirror can reflect large scenes, why cannot the mind? He denies that images are literally impressed in mirrors, arguing that seeing occurs by reflection, which can shift with perspective, proving it is not a real impression. Finally, he exposes the root error: the theologians equivocated on the term 'quiddity' (mahiyya), applying it both to the mental concept and to the external reality, then falsely assumed they are identical and share all attributes. The lesson thus reinforces the foundational distinction in Muslim epistemology between the two modes of existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental existence requires the mind to physically contain the object like a container.
Correction: Mental existence is only the intellectual form, and even in mirrors objects are not literally impressed but seen by reflection.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the mirror example show about mental existence?
A) That large objects can indeed be impressed in a small space
B) That images in mirrors are impressed just like mental forms
C) That even in mirrors impression is denied and seeing is by reflection
D) That mirrors cannot reflect large scenes

Key Takeaway
Large objects can be known because mental existence holds only the form, not the physical size; even mirror images are not impressed, proving the point.

?

How does confusing the two affect your contentment and your pursuit of what truly matters? What would it take for you to seek the reality (al-ḥaqīqa) rather than its shadow?
?
The mirror analogy shows that what you see is not the actual object but a reflection. In your life of worship, do you ever mistake the outward form—prayer, charity, fasting—for the reality of devotion? Are you content with a reflection of faith, or are you restless until you encounter the original? This lesson demands you examine: where are you settling for shadow existence?
?
The proof from the mirror demonstrates that when you move, the image shifts. Consider your own spiritual journey: have your beliefs and practices shifted with every changing circumstance, like a fleeting reflection? Or are you anchored to the unchanging Truth that lies beyond all reflections? What is one concrete step you will take today to distinguish the shadow from the substance in your faith?

The Root Error: Homonymy of 'Quiddity'
Exposes the homonymy of 'quiddity' as the root of the objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the objections against mental existence arise from equivocating on the word 'quiddity', which applies to both the mental concept and the external reality.

Core Idea
The word 'quiddity' has two meanings; confusing them causes false conclusions.

Having answered the two objections, the commentator reveals the deeper cause: the theologians who raised these objections mistakenly assumed that 'quiddity' refers to one single thing, when in fact it is used homonymously for the mental concept and the external existent. This lesson explains that error and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to the explanation of homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ).
2
Understand the homonymy point
The word 'quiddity' (mahiyya) applies to the intelligible thing existing mentally and also to what corresponds to it externally. They are not identical.
3
See the sky example
The commentator gives the example of the sky: its identity has greatness that prevents it from occurring in the mind, but its quiddity (as a concept) has no such barrier.
4
Reflect on the fallacy
The objectors supposed the two meanings are one and built their objections on that assumption, leading to falsity. This shows the importance of precise terminology in kalam.

Pure obstinacy. (Answer to the first objection:) It would only necessitate the mind being white and black if the identity of blackness and whiteness occurred in it—i.e., their quiddity existing by external existence called external existence, which is the source of external effects and manifestation of judgments—not their quiddity existing by shadow existence called mental existence. For you have learned in the discussions of mental existence that there is no meaning for quiddity except the intellectual form characterized by non-original existence. And you also learned that it (the intellectual form) differs from external identities characterized by original existences in the concomitants that pertain to the external existent by virtue of a particular role in them, as you have noted before. The location being black and white, as well as opposition, are of the type in which external existence has a role, so it does not necessitate the mind being characterized by what is definitely denied of it, nor the conjunction of two opposites. (And) the answer to the second objection: that it is impossible for the identity of the mountain to occur in a specific thing that has a specific proportionality such that it becomes a mirror for observing it—rather, it is like taking the form of an elephant in a grain of copper. I said: In this there is an admission that what occurs is not the quiddity of the mountain, and that is what is required. And by this the answer becomes clear to what was said: that the image of the mountain and the sky is impressed in the mirror despite its smallness—only that impression in it is denied. His saying (peace be upon him): (And the answer to the first, etc.) And it may be answered by the distinction between obtaining in the mind and obtaining in the locus: the first is circumstantial and the second is attributive. But that is nothing because the obtaining of the form in the mind necessitates the attribute of being knowing. Indeed, that would be complete if knowledge were other than the obtained form. His saying: (And the answer to the second objection, etc.) The summary of the two answers is the distinction between the two existences, external and shadowy: that the effects of opposition, greatness, and smallness, and the attribute of what is denied of the mind, are specific to external existence. And what is said: that this answer does not apply if the problem is brought concerning the concomitants of quiddity—it is repelled because by external existence is meant the original, and there is no doubt that the concomitants of quiddity have an original existence on which the effects follow, and a shadow existence, so the distinction between the two existences applies in them as well. This is refuted by the mirror, for it imprints nearly half the sphere of the universe, so the claim of necessity in its falsity is not heard. And it is answered by denying the impression of the visible in the mirror; rather, seeing by it is by way of reflection. The proof is that one who sees the image of something in front of the mirror at a specific spot, then moves from his place to another place without the mirror or what faces it moving, sees that image in another spot of the mirror. If the image were impressed, that would be impossible. Refuting the Philosophers' Definition of Knowledge as Abstraction
The commentator refutes the privative definition and offers an existential one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge cannot be defined merely as abstraction from matter, and what the correct definition is according to the commentator.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not just the removal of matter; it is the active presence of an immaterial form in an immaterial mind.

The philosophers proposed that knowledge is the abstraction of both knower and known from matter. The commentator shows this leads to an absurd consequence: if the soul is already immaterial, then everyone would know all immaterial things. Instead, he offers a more precise definition: knowledge is the obtaining of an immaterial form in an immaterial essence. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies the place of sense perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: definition, refutation, alternative, clarification.
2
Understand the refutation
Why does the abstraction definition lead to absurdity? Because if the soul is already immaterial, then everyone would know all immaterial things automatically. This is false.
The absurdity shows the definition is not just incomplete but invalid.
3
Examine the alternative definition
The commentator says knowledge is 'the obtaining of an immaterial form in an immaterial essence'. How does this avoid the earlier problem?
It adds a positive element: the form must actually be present, not just absent matter.
4
Consider the role of sense perception
The commentator explicitly excludes sense perceptions from this definition. Why is that acceptable? Because the definition is about intellections, not sensations.
This shows the definition is specific to rational knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما جعلوه) أي الحكماء العلم أمراً) عدمياً فقالوا : هو تجرد العالم والمعلوم من المادة) ، ورد بأنه يلزم منه أن يكون كل شخص إنساني عالماً بجميع المجردات فإنَّ النفس الإنسانية مجردة عندهم، وأقرب من هذا ما قيل : إن العلم حصول صورة مجردة المادة عند ذات مجردة عنها، ولابأس بخروج إدراكات الحواس عن تعريف العلم، لأن الكلام في التعقلات دون الإحساسات كما دلت عليه المباحث السابقة، قال الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية : قد اضطرب كلام ابن سينا في حقيقة العلم فحيث بين أن
عن
قوله : ( وأقرب من (هذا أي من قولهم وهو تجرد العالم والمعلوم لاختصاص كل منهما بالتعقل، إلا أنه على الأول ،عدمي وعلى الثاني وجودي بخلاف ما مر من أنه الموجود الذهني، فإنه شامل للأنواع الأربعة للعلم .
