Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all powers that produce multiple acts are the same, but the text distinguishes between volitional and non-volitional multiplicity.
Correction: The second interpretation focuses on multiplicity alone, while the first requires will. Animal power meets both, plant soul only meets the second.

Checkpoint
Why is the celestial soul considered power only under the first interpretation and not the second?
A) Because it lacks consciousness of its acts.
B) Because it is not a near principle for different acts.
C) Because it acts without will.
D) Because it is a substance, not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
The classification of power depends on whether the entity acts with will and whether it produces multiple distinct actions; these two criteria yield four categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you act with conscious will (iraada) like the celestial and animal powers, or are you merely going through the motions like a natural force? Examine one habit in your life: is it driven by deliberate intention for Allah or by mechanical routine?
?
The animal power combines will and multiple acts. In your worship, do you aim for both sincere will (ikhlas) and diverse good deeds? Or do you focus on quantity of actions without checking your heart's intention?
?
This classification shows that lack of consciousness disqualifies a power from being true qudra. When you perform acts of obedience, are you spiritually awake (shu'ur) to what you are doing, or are you heedless (ghafla)? What will you do today to increase your consciousness in a single action?

The Four Types of Effective Attributes
Al-Ījī enumerates the four types of effective attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how effective attributes are classified into four types based on consciousness and multiplicity.

Core Idea
Effective attributes are either conscious or not, and a principle of one or many acts.

Al-Ījī, drawing from al-Mulakhkhas, presents a precise classification of effective attributes (al-ṣifa al-muʾaththira). The taxonomy uses two dichotomies: consciousness and the number of acts produced. This yields four mutually exclusive categories, each with a distinct example from cosmology and natural philosophy. Let’s examine the classification directly from the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the two criteria mentioned at the start.
The passage is concise; each word is significant.
2
Identify the two criteria
The classification uses two binary criteria: shāʿira (conscious) vs. ghayr shāʿira, and mabdaʾ li-fiʿl wāḥid (principle of one act) vs. mabdaʾ li-afʿāl kathīra (principle of many acts). Notice how these combine.
3
List the four types
The passage names four types: celestial soul, elemental nature, animal power, and vegetative soul. For each, infer which combination of criteria applies. Check your understanding with the commentary.
The celestial soul is conscious and a principle of one act, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل المذكور في الملخص أن الصفة المؤثرة إما شاعرة أم لا، وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون مبدأ لفعل واحد أو لأفعال كثيرة، فالقسم الأول النفس الفلكية، والثاني الطبيعية العنصرية، والثالث القوة الحيوانية، والرابع النفس النباتية، وليس في الملخص دلالة على اعتبار قيد القرب في المبدأ والتأثير والأولى تركه ثم لا يتعين كون النسخة المشهورة سهواً، لاحتمال أن يكون مبنياً على اعتبار أنَّ المؤثر هو النفس النباتية والكيفيات آلات لها، إلا أن يثبت من المصنف أن القرب في التعريف احتراز عن النفوس النباتية إذ لا توجد فائدة للقرب سواه .
The summary of what is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas is that the effective attribute is either conscious or not, and in both cases either it is a principle of one act or of many acts. So the first kind is the celestial soul, the second is the elemental nature, the third is the animal power, and the fourth is the vegetative soul. And in al-Mulakhkhas there is no indication of considering the condition of proximity (qurb) in the principle and effect, and it is better to omit it. Then it is not certain that the well-known copy is a mistake, because it is possible that it is based on considering that the effective agent is the vegetative soul and the qualities are instruments for it, unless it is established from the author that proximity in the definition is a safeguard against the vegetative souls, since there is no benefit for proximity other than that.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage classifies effective attributes (al-ṣifa al-muʾaththira) into four types. The classification is based on two binary criteria: whether the attribute is conscious (shāʿira) or not, and whether it is the principle (mabdaʾ) of one act or many acts. The author states the summary from al-Mulakhkhas: the first kind is the celestial soul (al-nafs al-falakiyya), which is conscious and a principle of one act. The second is the elemental nature (al-ṭabīʿiyya al-ʿunṣuriyya), which is not conscious and a principle of one act. The third is the animal power (al-quwwa al-ḥayawāniyya), which is conscious and a principle of many acts. The fourth is the vegetative soul (al-nafs al-nabātiyya), which is not conscious but a principle of many acts. The author then notes that al-Mulakhkhas does not require the condition of proximity (qurb) in the principle or effect, and it is better to omit it. He also discusses a textual issue: the well-known copy might not be a scribal error, because it could be based on the view that the vegetative soul is the effective agent and qualities are mere instruments. The condition of proximity would then be a safeguard to exclude vegetative souls from the definition of power.

D) Vegetative soul

Key Takeaway
Effective attributes divide into four types based on two binary criteria: consciousness and the number of acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson classifies effective attributes into four types. Consider your own actions: are they more like the single, non-conscious effect of fire, or the many, conscious movements of an animal? What does that reveal about your sense of purpose and intentionality?
?
The celestial soul is conscious but produces only one act. Have you ever felt that your life is focused on a single overarching goal? If so, is that goal truly conscious and chosen, and are you as constant in it as the celestial rotation?
?
The vegetative soul produces many effects without awareness. Are there areas of your life where you act without reflection—habits, routines, or patterns—that still produce results? Could bringing awareness to those areas transform them from mere nature into conscious action?

The Condition of Proximity and Textual Variants
Al-Ījī discusses the condition of proximity and a textual variant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of proximity (qurb) is debated and how a variant reading in al-Mulakhkhas affects the classification.

Core Idea
Proximity may be a safeguard to exclude vegetative souls from the definition of power.

After presenting the four types, the author addresses a subtle point: whether the definition of effective attribute should include the condition that the principle is 'proximate' (qurb). He notes that al-Mulakhkhas does not mention this condition and that omitting it is preferable. Then he discusses a textual variant that might explain why some copies include it. Let’s read the passage and unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the author's stance on the condition of proximity and the mention of a textual variant.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يتعين كون النسخة المشهورة سهواً'.
2
Understand the issue of proximity
The author argues that al-Mulakhkhas does not indicate the condition of proximity (qurb) in the principle and effect, and it is better to omit it. Ask yourself: why might someone include it?
3
Analyse the textual argument
The author considers a possible interpretation: the well-known copy might not be a mistake if it is based on the view that the vegetative soul is the effective agent and qualities are instruments. Proximity would then safeguard against including vegetative souls. Notice how the author leaves the issue open unless the original author's intent is proven.
This shows the depth of kalām textual analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The summary of what is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas is that the effective attribute is either conscious or not, and in both cases either it is a principle of one act or of many acts. ?

The Definition of Ability and the Issue of Proximity
Classification of forces and critique of including proximity in definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of proximity (qurb) is not essential in the definition of ability and how the classification of forces relates to this issue.

Core Idea
Proximity is not a necessary condition for defining ability.

In defining ability (qudra), scholars debated whether it must be a proximate source of acts. The passage before us lists different types of forces and argues that including proximity in the definition is unnecessary and better omitted. Let's see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on forces and proximity
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the list of four forces and the discussion about proximity.
The passage mentions celestial, elemental, animal, and vegetative forces.
2
Understand why proximity is not required
The passage says al-Mulakhkhas does not support proximity, and it is better to abandon it. Consider the alternative interpretation that proximity might exclude vegetative souls, but even then it is not beneficial.
The author leans toward omitting proximity from the definition.
3
Reflect on the classification of forces
Think about how these forces relate to ability: are they sources of acts? This prepares us for the next lesson on diverse acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلكية، والثاني الطبيعية العنصرية، والثالث القوة الحيوانية، والرابع النفس النباتية، وليس في الملخص دلالة على اعتبار قيد القرب في المبدأ والتأثير والأولى تركه ثم لا يتعين كون النسخة المشهورة سهواً، لاحتمال أن يكون مبنياً على اعتبار أن المؤثر هو النفس النباتية والكيفيات آلات لها، إلا أن يثبت من المصنف أن القرب في التعريف احتراز عن النفوس النباتية إذ لا توجد فائدة للقرب سواه. قوله: ( وليست أفعالها مختلفة ) إذ المراد من كون القدرة مبدأ للأفعال المختلفة، أن تكون مبدأ لفعل تارة أخرى لآخر، وليست القوى العنصرية كذلك، وإن كان تصدر عنها أفعال كاليبس والإحراق من النار مثلاً. قوله: ( القدرة الحادثة على رأينا ) أجاب عنه صاحب المقاصد بأن ليس المراد التأثير بالفعل بل بالقوة، بمعنى أنها صفة من شأنها التأثير والإيجاد على ما صرح به الآمدي، حيث قال: القدرة صفة وجودية من شأنها تأتي الإيجاد والإحداث بها على وجه يتصور ممن قامت به الفعل بدلاً عن الترك، والترك بدلاً عن الفعل والقدرة الحادثة كذلك، لكن لم تؤثر لوقوع متعلقها بقدرة الله تعالى.
Celestial, and second elemental natural, and third animal power, and fourth vegetative soul, and there is no indication in al-Mulakhkhas of considering the condition of proximity in the source and effect, and it is better to abandon it. Then it is not certain that the famous version is an error, because it is possible that it is based on considering that the effective agent is the vegetative soul and the qualities are its instruments, unless it is established from the author that proximity in the definition is a precaution against the vegetative souls, since there is no benefit to proximity other than that. He said: (And its acts are not different) that the meaning of ability being a source of different acts is that it is a source of one act at one time and another at another, and the elemental forces are not like that, even if acts like dryness and burning emanate from fire, for example. He said: (The created ability in our view) answered it by the author of al-Maqasid that the intended [meaning] is not effect in actuality but in potentiality, meaning that it is an attribute whose nature is to effect and originate, as al-Amidi stated, where he said: Ability is an existential attribute whose nature is to effect and originate such that the agent is imagined to perform the act instead of refraining, and refrain instead of performing, and the created ability is like that, but it did not effect because its object occurs by the power of God.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage clarifies the definition of ability (qudra) as a source of diverse acts and addresses an objection about created ability. It first lists types of forces—celestial, elemental natural, animal, and vegetative—and notes that the condition of proximity (qurb) in the definition is not supported in al-Mulakhkhas and is better abandoned. The passage then explains that the requirement that ability be a source of different acts means it must be capable of producing different acts at different times, which elemental forces (like fire that always produces heat) do not meet. Finally, it presents the response from the author of al-Maqasid: the created ability is not defined by actual effect (ta'thir bi al-fi'l) but by potential effect (ta'thir bi al-quwwa), meaning it is an attribute inherently suited to effect and originate, as al-Amidi defined it. This potential is present in created ability, but it does not actually effect because its object is already effected by God's power.

Key Terms
قدرة
قيد القرب
القوى العنصرية
نفس نباتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that proximity is an essential condition in the definition of ability.
Correction: The passage argues that al-Mulakhkhas does not support proximity, and it is better to abandon it. The only possible use is to exclude vegetative souls, but even then it is not beneficial.

Checkpoint

A) Celestial
B) Elemental natural
C) Divine
D) Animal

Key Takeaway
The definition of ability should not include proximity as a condition because it serves no clear purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage shows the author using careful textual reasoning from al-Mulakhkhas to critique an inherited definition. Are you willing to scrutinize your own religious convictions with the same rigor, even if it means challenging what you have always been taught?
?
The author considers a potential purpose for the proximity condition—excluding vegetative souls—but still deems it unnecessary. Is there anything in your faith that you justify with a 'possible' purpose, even though it lacks clear evidence? Will you commit to seeking truth over convenience?

Ability as Source of Diverse Acts and the Response on Created Ability
Clarification of diverse acts and response to created ability objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that created ability is defined by potential effect rather than actual diversity of acts.

Core Idea
Created ability is potential, not actual effect.

The passage continues by clarifying what it means for ability to be a source of different acts. It then addresses an objection about created ability: if created ability cannot produce diverse acts, how can it be called ability? The answer redefines ability as an attribute of potential effect, not actual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on diverse acts and the objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. First, the passage explains that ability must be a source of different acts at different times. Then an objection about created ability is raised and answered.
Pay attention to the key phrase 'ta'thir bi al-quwwa' (potential effect).
2
Understand the distinction between actual and potential effect
The response distinguishes between effect in actuality (би-л-фи'l) and effect in potentiality (би-л-кувва). Created ability has the potential to effect, but does not actualize because God does so.
This preserves the definition of ability while maintaining divine omnipotence.
3
Reflect on al-Amidi's definition
Al-Amidi defines ability as an existential attribute whose nature is to effect and originate, allowing for the possibility of acting or refraining. This applies to created ability as well.
This definition resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلكية، والثاني الطبيعية العنصرية، والثالث القوة الحيوانية، والرابع النفس النباتية، وليس في الملخص دلالة على اعتبار قيد القرب في المبدأ والتأثير والأولى تركه ثم لا يتعين كون النسخة المشهورة سهواً، لاحتمال أن يكون مبنياً على اعتبار أن المؤثر هو النفس النباتية والكيفيات آلات لها، إلا أن يثبت من المصنف أن القرب في التعريف احتراز عن النفوس النباتية إذ لا توجد فائدة للقرب سواه. قوله: ( وليست أفعالها مختلفة ) إذ المراد من كون القدرة مبدأ للأفعال المختلفة، أن تكون مبدأ لفعل تارة أخرى لآخر، وليست القوى العنصرية كذلك، وإن كان تصدر عنها أفعال كاليبس والإحراق من النار مثلاً. قوله: ( القدرة الحادثة على رأينا ) أجاب عنه صاحب المقاصد بأن ليس المراد التأثير بالفعل بل بالقوة، بمعنى أنها صفة من شأنها التأثير والإيجاد على ما صرح به الآمدي، حيث قال: القدرة صفة وجودية من شأنها تأتي الإيجاد والإحداث بها على وجه يتصور ممن قامت به الفعل بدلاً عن الترك، والترك بدلاً عن الفعل والقدرة الحادثة كذلك، لكن لم تؤثر لوقوع متعلقها بقدرة الله تعالى.
Celestial, and second elemental natural, and third animal power, and fourth vegetative soul, and there is no indication in al-Mulakhkhas of considering the condition of proximity in the source and effect, and it is better to abandon it. Then it is not certain that the famous version is an error, because it is possible that it is based on considering that the effective agent is the vegetative soul and the qualities are its instruments, unless it is established from the author that proximity in the definition is a precaution against the vegetative souls, since there is no benefit to proximity other than that. He said: (And its acts are not different) that the meaning of ability being a source of different acts is that it is a source of one act at one time and another at another, and the elemental forces are not like that, even if acts like dryness and burning emanate from fire, for example. He said: (The created ability in our view) answered it by the author of al-Maqasid that the intended [meaning] is not effect in actuality but in potentiality, meaning that it is an attribute whose nature is to effect and originate, as al-Amidi stated, where he said: Ability is an existential attribute whose nature is to effect and originate such that the agent is imagined to perform the act instead of refraining, and refrain instead of performing, and the created ability is like that, but it did not effect because its object occurs by the power of God.

?

Defining Power: Consensus and Dissent
Consensus on power as existential and Bishr's non-existential view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mainstream definition of power (qudra) as an existential attribute and the alternative non-existential view advanced by Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir.

Core Idea
Power is an existential attribute that makes both acting and refraining possible.

Before diving into detailed theology on divine attributes, we need to agree on what 'power' means for created beings. Al-Jurjani lays out the consensus view that power is an actually existing quality, and then presents a dissenting opinion that reduces power to merely the absence of defects. This passage sets the stage for debates on whether power is an added entity or just a condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the translation. Notice the structure: consensus, then dissenting view, then a famous follower of that view.
Pay attention to the technical terms: 'ṣifa wujūdiyya' and 'ṣifa ʿadamiyya'.
2
Identify the three positions
Find and list the consensus definition, Bishr's definition, and al-Razi's choice. Understand how each defines power.
The consensus says power is existential; Bishr says it is non-existential (absence of defects).
3
Understand the implications of 'existential vs non-existential'
Reflect on why this distinction matters. If power is existential, it is an added attribute; if non-existential, it is merely the lack of obstacles. This affects how we think about God's power and free will.
Bishr's view reduces power to a condition, not a separate quality.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
Consider why al-Razi, a major Ashʿarite theologian, adopted the Muʿtazilite Bishr's view. This shows that even within schools, there is nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفقت الأشاعرة والمعتزلة وغيرهم على أنَّ القدرة صفة وجودية يتأتى معها الفعل بدلاً عن الترك، والترك بدلاً عن الفعل وقال بشر بن المعتمر: القدرة الحادثة عبارة عن سلامة البنية عن الآفات فجعلها صفة عدمية قال: فمن أثبت صفة زائدة على سلامة البنية فعليه البرهان واختار الإمام الرازي في المحصل مذهبه
The Ashʿarites, Muʿtazilites, and others agreed that power (al-qudra) is an existential attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya) with which the act is possible in place of refraining, and refraining in place of the act. And Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir said: The originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) is [nothing] but the soundness of the constitution (salāma al-binya) from defects; thus he made it a non-existential attribute (ṣifa ʿadamiyya). He said: Whoever affirms an additional attribute beyond soundness of constitution, the proof is upon him. And Imam al-Rāzī chose his school in al-Muḥaṣṣal.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage states the agreement of the Ashʿarites, Muʿtazilites, and others that power (qudra) is an existential attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya), meaning it is an actually existing quality that makes the act possible as an alternative to refraining, and refraining as an alternative to the act. Then it mentions the dissenting view of Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir, who said that originated power (qudra ḥāditha) is simply the soundness of the bodily constitution (salāma al-binya) from defects, making it a non-existential attribute (ṣifa ʿadamiyya) – i.e., the absence of defects. Bishr challenged: whoever affirms an additional (existential) attribute beyond this soundness must provide proof. The passage notes that Imam al-Rāzī chose Bishr's view in his book al-Muḥaṣṣal.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
صفة وجودية (ṣifa wujūdiyya)
صفة عدمية (ṣifa ʿadamiyya)
سلامة البنية (salāma al-binya)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power is only the ability to act, not to refrain.
Correction: The consensus definition explicitly states that power makes both the act and refraining possible, i.e., it is the ability to choose either.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir's definition of originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)?
A) It is an existential attribute enabling action and refraining.
B) It is the soundness of the constitution from defects.
C) It is an additional attribute created by God.
D) It is the ability to act in place of refraining.

Key Takeaway
Power is defined by the majority as an existential attribute enabling both action and inaction, but some reduce it to merely the absence of physical defects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the times you have failed to do something good or succeeded in avoiding harm, do you attribute that to your own power or to a deeper gift from God? How does the existential view of power challenge you to see every action and refrain as dependent on a real attribute given by Him?
?
Al-Razi, a towering scholar, chose a minority view on power. Are there beliefs you hold simply because they are popular among your teachers or peers, without personally examining the evidence? What would it cost you to honestly re-evaluate one such belief today?
?
If power were merely the absence of defects, as Bishr claimed, then you would have no real ability to choose—only the lack of impediment. In your own life, have you ever experienced a situation where your physical health was fine, yet you still could not act virtuously? What does that reveal about the true nature of power?

Knowing Originated Power Through Immediate Awareness
Al-Jurjani presents the Ash'ari proof for originated power via immediate awareness and provides two illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris prove the existence of originated power through immediate awareness (wijdān) and the examples that illustrate this.

Core Idea
We know our power exists by direct experience, not by deduction.

The Ash'ari position on proving human power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) relies on an intuitive awareness that everyone can verify. This lesson introduces the method of immediate awareness and the two key examples of ascending by choice versus falling involuntarily, and voluntary movement versus tremors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: first a statement of the method, then examples.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage gives two examples: ascending vs falling, and voluntary movement vs tremor. Reflect on how each pair shows a distinction that points to an underlying power.
These examples are meant to be self-evident.
3
Understand 'wijdān' as immediate awareness
Recognize that the proof does not rely on abstract reasoning but on a direct, necessary awareness. Ask yourself: Can you directly feel the difference between moving your hand voluntarily and it trembling?
This awareness is what the Ash'aris call wijdān.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : إثبات القدرة الحادثة وتعريفها اختلف في طريق إثباتها أي إثبات القدرة الحادثة والعلم بها والحق ما عليه الأشاعرة وهو أنها تعرف ويعلم وجودها بالوجدان كما أشرنا إليه حيث قلنا: إن الفرق بين الصاعد بالاختيار والساقط عن علو ضروري، فإنا نجد حالة الصعود أمراً ثابتاً لها دون حالة السقوط، وكذا نجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار وقال الهمداني من المعتزلة هو أي طريق إثباتها تأتي الفعل قوله : ( وكذا تجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار اعترض عليه الإمام بأن الاختيار قبل الفعل باطل عندكم ومعه ممنوع لامتناع العدم حال الوجود وأيضاً حصول الحركة حال ما خلقها الله ضروري وقبله محال فأين الاختيار وأجيب بأن الضروري هو التفرقة بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة الاختيار مع قطع النظر عن الأمور
The Fourth Objective: Proving the Originated Power and Its Definition. There is disagreement regarding the method of proving it (i.e., proving the originated power and knowing it). And the truth is what the Ashʿarites hold, which is that it is known and its existence is known through immediate awareness (wijdān) as we have indicated, where we said: The difference between the one ascending by choice and the one falling from a height is necessary [and] self-evident, for we find the state of ascending as an established thing for it without the state of falling. Likewise, we find a necessary distinction between the movement of tremor (ḥarakat al-irtiʿāsh) and voluntary movement (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār). And al-Hamadānī from the Muʿtazila said: The method of proving it is the occuring of the act. He said [the commentator says]: 'And similarly we find a necessary distinction between the movement of tremor and voluntary movement.' The Imām [al-Rāzī] objected: 'Choice (al-ikhtiyār) before the act is invalid according to you, and with it is prevented because of the impossibility of non-existence (al-ʿadam) during existence. Also, the occurrence of movement when God creates it is necessary, and before it is impossible; so where is choice?' And the answer was: 'The necessary [thing] is the distinction in the sense of capability (al-tamakkun) of the act and refraining, with consideration of the voluntary movement itself apart from other matters.'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from the fourth section deals with proving the existence of originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) in humans. It states that there is disagreement on the method of proof, and the truth according to the Ashʿarites is that it is known through immediate awareness (wijdān). The proof they provide is the necessary distinction we perceive between ascending voluntarily and falling involuntarily: we find that the state of ascending involves an established factor (power) that is absent in the state of falling. Similarly, we find a necessary distinction between trembling (ḥarakat al-irtiʿāsh) and voluntary movement (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār). The Muʿtazilite al-Hamadānī instead held that the method of proof is the occurrence of the act itself. Then the commentator records an objection from Imam al-Rāzī: if choice (ikhtiyār) is considered before the act, it is invalid according to you (Ashʿarites?), and if it is simultaneous with the act, then it is prevented because non-existence cannot coexist with existence. Also, the movement occurs when God creates it necessarily, and before that it is impossible, so where is choice? The answer given is that the necessary distinction is in terms of capability (tamakkun) of the act and refraining, considering the voluntary movement itself apart from other considerations (i.e., the capability is what is perceived, not a separate power before the act).

Key Terms
الوجدان (al-wijdān)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
حركة الارتعاش (ḥarakat al-irtiʿāsh)
حركة الاختيار (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof relies on observing external acts and inferring a power behind them, but the proof is immediate awareness, not inference.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that we directly find (najidu) the distinction, not infer it.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view in this passage, how is originated power known?
A) By logical deduction.
B) By immediate awareness (wijdān).
C) By revelation.
D) By consensus.

Key Takeaway
Originated power is known through direct, immediate awareness, not through inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a voluntary action—like raising your hand—do you directly sense your own power in that moment, or have you become so habituated that you no longer notice it? This lesson says the distinction is self-evident. If it is not self-evident to you, what has dulled your awareness?
?
Consider a time you acted out of habit rather than choice. Could you have stopped yourself? What does that tell you about the reality of your originated power? Commit today to pause before one voluntary action and consciously acknowledge the power behind it.
?
If your originated power is known only through immediate awareness, what happens when you ignore that awareness? Are you at risk of attributing your actions to mere circumstance? This lesson calls you to own your choices as expressions of God-given power. Will you accept that call?

Objection and Answer: Capability (Tamakkun) as the Basis of Proof
Al-Jurjani records al-Razi's objection to the wijdān proof and the Ash'ari response clarifying capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection to the wijdān proof and the Ash'ari response that the perceived distinction is one of capability (tamakkun) concurrent with the act.

Core Idea
The perceived distinction is not prior choice but simultaneous capability.

After presenting the proof via immediate awareness, the text records an objection from Imam al-Razi and the Ash'ari response. This lesson explains the objection that choice cannot exist before or with the act, and the answer that what is directly perceived is the capability (tamakkun) to act or refrain, which is simultaneous with the movement.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read al-Razi's two-part objection: choice before the act is invalid, and choice with the act is impossible because non-existence cannot coexist with existence. Also, the movement occurs when God creates it.
Notice that al-Razi is questioning where choice fits in.
2
Identify the Ash'ari response
The answer shifts the focus from a separate entity called choice to the notion of capability (tamakkun) that is directly perceived within the voluntary movement itself.
This resolves the objection by redefining what is directly known.
3
Understand capability as concurrent
Capability is not a power existing before the act nor a separate entity during the act, but the very fact that the movement is under one's control is perceived as a distinction. This preserves the immediate awareness proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : إثبات القدرة الحادثة وتعريفها اختلف في طريق إثباتها أي إثبات القدرة الحادثة والعلم بها والحق ما عليه الأشاعرة وهو أنها تعرف ويعلم وجودها بالوجدان كما أشرنا إليه حيث قلنا: إن الفرق بين الصاعد بالاختيار والساقط عن علو ضروري، فإنا نجد حالة الصعود أمراً ثابتاً لها دون حالة السقوط، وكذا نجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار وقال الهمداني من المعتزلة هو أي طريق إثباتها تأتي الفعل قوله : ( وكذا تجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار اعترض عليه الإمام بأن الاختيار قبل الفعل باطل عندكم ومعه ممنوع لامتناع العدم حال الوجود وأيضاً حصول الحركة حال ما خلقها الله ضروري وقبله محال فأين الاختيار وأجيب بأن الضروري هو التفرقة بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة الاختيار مع قطع النظر عن الأمور
The Fourth Objective: Proving the Originated Power and Its Definition. There is disagreement regarding the method of proving it (i.e., proving the originated power and knowing it). And the truth is what the Ashʿarites hold, which is that it is known and its existence is known through immediate awareness (wijdān) as we have indicated, where we said: The difference between the one ascending by choice and the one falling from a height is necessary [and] self-evident, for we find the state of ascending as an established thing for it without the state of falling. Likewise, we find a necessary distinction between the movement of tremor (ḥarakat al-irtiʿāsh) and voluntary movement (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār). And al-Hamadānī from the Muʿtazila said: The method of proving it is the occuring of the act. Also, the occurrence of movement when God creates it is necessary, and before it is impossible; so where is choice?' And the answer was: 'The necessary [thing] is the distinction in the sense of capability (al-tamakkun) of the act and refraining, with consideration of the voluntary movement itself apart from other matters.'
Source pages p. 91

This passage from the fourth section deals with proving the existence of originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) in humans. It states that there is disagreement on the method of proof, and the truth according to the Ashʿarites is that it is known through immediate awareness (wijdān). The Muʿtazilite al-Hamadānī instead held that the method of proof is the occurrence of the act itself. Then the commentator records an objection from Imam al-Rāzī: if choice (ikhtiyār) is considered before the act, it is invalid according to you (Ashʿarites?), and if it is simultaneous with the act, then it is prevented because non-existence cannot coexist with existence. Also, the movement occurs when God creates it necessarily, and before that it is impossible, so where is choice? The answer given is that the necessary distinction is in terms of capability (tamakkun) of the act and refraining, considering the voluntary movement itself apart from other considerations (i.e., the capability is what is perceived, not a separate power before the act).

Key Terms
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār)
التمكن (al-tamakkun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the wijdān proof is refuted by al-Razi's objection, but the response shows that it remains valid by clarifying that what is perceived is capability, not a separate prior power.
Correction: The answer redefines the direct awareness as capability (tamakkun), which is concurrent with the act.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari answer to al-Razi's objection?
A) Choice exists before the act.
B) The necessary distinction is in the sense of capability (tamakkun) of the act and refraining.
C) The proof is based on the act's occurrence.
D) Al-Razi's objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The wijdān proof withstands al-Razi's objection by identifying the directly perceived factor as capability (tamakkun), not a prior choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a voluntary action, do you directly perceive that you could have done otherwise? How does this lesson challenge your understanding of that feeling? Commit to reflecting on one action today where you truly felt capable of both doing and refraining.
?
Al-Razi argued that choice cannot exist before or with the act. Have you ever felt that your choices were illusory? How does the Ash'ari concept of tamakkun (capability) change your view of your own agency? Write down one area of your life where you need to affirm your capability to choose, even if the outcome depends on God.
?
This lesson distinguishes between having a prior choice and having simultaneous capability. Which do you normally assume you possess? Take two minutes in silence, examine a recent decision, and ask yourself: Did I really have a separate power, or was it simply the possibility of alternatives? How does this affect your gratitude to Allah for enabling your actions?

Definition of Power and Two Deviant Views
Defines created power and presents two deviant views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of created power and the two minority views that reduce power to limb capability or to the act itself.

Core Idea
Power is not merely a sound limb; it is an attribute distinct from the body.

The concept of power (qudra) is central to discussions of human action. In this passage, the author defines power in relation to us and presents two reductive views that later theologians rejected. Let's examine the text to see how the author distinguishes the sound position from these deviations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then the English translation. Notice the brief definition and the two opinions that follow.
The passage is short but packed with three positions: a baseline definition and two alternative views.
2
Understand the main definition
Identify the statement about 'reference in power in our right'. The author says that if power refers to soundness of limbs, it's intelligible; otherwise there is dispute. This sets the stage for the debate.
This is a key distinction: the orthodox view does NOT reduce power to limbs, but allows for a different meaning.
3
Analyse the view of Durar and Hisham
Note that Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim defined power as 'a part of the powerful' (ba'd al-qadir). They gave examples: power to grasp is a sound hand, power to walk is a sound foot. This reduces power to physical capability.
This view is considered deviant because it denies that power is an attribute distinct from the body.
4
Examine the rejected view
The text then mentions another opinion that power is 'a part of the empowerable' (ba'd al-maqdur), meaning something about the act itself. The author dismisses it as obviously false.
No explanation is given for its falsity; it is considered self-evidently wrong.
5
Reflect and summarise
Consider why the orthodox Ash'ari position insists that power is an attribute, not a limb or part of the act. Write down your own one-sentence summary of the correct view.
This reflection will help you see why the two alternative views were rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الرابع : إثبات القدرة الحادثة وتعريفها حيث قال : المرجع بالقدرة في حقنا إن كان إلى سلامة الأعضاء، فهو معقول، وإن كان إلى أمر آخر ففيه النزاع ) وقال ضرار بن عمرو وهشام بن سالم : إنها أي القدرة الحادثة ( بعض القادر) فالقدرة على الأخذ عبارة عن اليد السليمة والقدرة على المشي عبارة عن الرجل السليمة ( وقيل : ( القدرة ( بعض المقدور ) وفساده أظهر من أن يخفى .
The fourth type - the fourth objective: proof of the created power and its definition. He said: The reference in power in our right if it refers to soundness of limbs, then it is intelligible, and if it refers to something else, then there is dispute. And Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim said: it [i.e., the created power] is a part of the powerful (ba'd al-qadir). So the power to take is an expression of the sound hand, and the power to walk is an expression of the sound foot. And it was said: power is a part of the empowerable (ba'd al-maqdur), and its falsity is more apparent than to be hidden.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of power (al-qudra) in relation to human beings and presents two minority views. The text begins by stating that if we refer power in our right to soundness of limbs, then that is understandable, but if it refers to something else, then there is dispute. It then reports the opinion of Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim, who held that power is a part of the powerful (some attribute of the agent). For example, the power to grasp is simply a sound hand, and the power to walk is simply a sound foot. This view reduces power to mere physical capability. Then it mentions another opinion that power is a part of the empowerable (something about the act). The text dismisses this view as obviously false.

Key Terms
القدرة
ضرار بن عمرو
هشام بن سالم
بعض القادر
بعض المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is identical to the soundness of a limb; e.g., the power to grasp is simply a healthy hand.
Correction: The Ash'ari view distinguishes power as an attribute that is not reducible to bodily health, otherwise amputees would lack any power.

Checkpoint
According to Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim, what is power?
A) An attribute separable from the body
B) A part of the powerful, e.g., a sound hand for grasping
C) A part of the empowerable act
D) A spiritual capability

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position distinguishes power as an attribute, not reducible to limbs or acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something difficult, do you ever feel that your ability is simply the strength of your body, or do you recognize an invisible gift from Allah? In the next 24 hours, try to catch yourself attributing success to your own limbs alone and consciously shift your gratitude to the One who granted you the capacity.
?
The deviant views in this lesson reduce power to either limbs or actions. Have you ever acted as if your power comes solely from your physical capabilities, ignoring the Creator who sustains them? What specific change in your daily routine could remind you that your power is a borrowed attribute?
?
This lesson teaches that power is an attribute distinct from the act. Is there an area of your life where you feel powerless, perhaps because you think your physical or mental abilities are 'just not enough'? How would your perspective shift if you believed that Allah can grant you a power beyond your current capacity? Commit to making dua for one such area today.

The Flaw in al-Jubba'i's Definition of Knowing Power
Author refutes al-Jubba'i's definition of the way of knowing power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jubba'i's definition of the way of knowing power is insufficient, and how the author refutes it with a counterexample.

Core Idea
Knowing someone is healthy doesn't tell you if they're currently able to act.

Al-Jubba'i, a Mu'tazilite theologian, proposed that we know a person has power (qudra) by knowing they are sound in body and free from defects. The author of *Sharh al-Mawaqif* rejects this definition with a simple but powerful counterexample: a person can be sound yet be asleep or incapacitated, and thus have no power. This short passage is a model of concise philosophical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the author first states al-Jubba'i's view, then gives a counterexample, then emphasizes the consensus.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the logical flaw
The definition claims that soundness entails power. The author shows that soundness can exist without power (e.g., sleep, incapacity). Therefore, the definition fails.
Think about why this is a decisive objection.
3
Reflect on the broader significance
This objection upholds the Ash'ari view that power is not simply a matter of physical health. Power is a separate attribute that can be absent even when the body is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علي ( الجبائي : هو ( أي طريق العلم بالقدرة ( العلم بصحة الشخص) وسلامته عن الآفات (قلنا: قد توجد تلك الصحة للشخص ) ولا قدرة له عند اتصافه بأضدادها من النوم والعجز فلا يكون العلم بتلك الصحة مستلزماً للعلم بثبوت القدرة، كيف والصحيح المتصف بتلك الأضداد لا قدرة له (اجماعاً) .
Al-Jubba'i: It (i.e., the way of knowing power) is knowledge of a person's soundness and freedom from defects. We said: That soundness may be found in a person, and he has no power when he is characterized by its opposites like sleep and incapacity, so knowledge of that soundness does not entail knowledge of the existence of power. How could it, when the sound person characterized by those opposites has no power by consensus?
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Jubba'i's definition of the way of knowing power. The author first states al-Jubba'i's view: the way to know that a person has power is to know that they are sound in body and free from defects. Then the author objects with a counterexample: a person may be sound yet have no power at a given moment because they are asleep or incapacitated. Therefore, knowing soundness does not necessarily entail knowing power. The author emphasizes that even a sound person who is asleep or incapacitated has no power by scholarly consensus, so the definition fails.

Key Terms
طريق العلم بالقدرة
صحة الشخص
الآفات
النوم
العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often assume that if someone is physically sound and healthy, they must be capable of performing actions at any moment.
Correction: This passage shows that soundness does not guarantee current ability; sleep and incapacity are clear counterexamples.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject al-Jubba'i's definition of the way of knowing power?
A) Because soundness is not necessary for power.
B) Because a sound person may lack power due to sleep or incapacity.
C) Because power can only be known through revelation.
D) Because al-Jubba'i was a Mu'tazili.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's definition fails because even a healthy person can lack power due to sleep or incapacity, so soundness is not a reliable indicator of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assess your own ability to act, do you instinctively look at your physical health and soundness? Where in your life have you assumed that because you are 'fine', you are capable—only to be humbled by sleep, illness, or unexpected weakness? How might this lesson reshape the way you honestly evaluate your own power?
?
Consider the subtle arrogance of thinking your health guarantees your abilities. If even a sound person can be powerless at any moment, what does that say about your true dependency on Allah for every action? Take a specific area of your life today—your work, worship, or relationships—and ask: Am I trusting in my own soundness or in the One who gives power moment by moment? Commit to one concrete reminder of that dependency.
?
Al-Jubba'i's mistake was assuming soundness and power are inseparable. What is a personal mistake you have made in assuming that an outward condition (health, status, resources) equals inward capability? How can you use this lesson to correct that pattern in your heart and actions starting now?

Is Inability the Opposite of Ability?
Discusses the nature of inability (al-ʿajz) and whether it is the opposite of ability (al-qudra).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opinions on whether inability (al-ʿajz) is the opposite of ability (al-qudra) and the argument about relating to the non-existent.

Core Idea
Inability can mean either lack of power or a positive attribute that prevents action.

This lesson examines a fundamental question in kalam: is inability simply the absence of ability, or is it a real attribute that is its opposite? The discussion involves two opinions, with the second supported by consensus on the trembling person. We will also see how the term 'inability' is used in two different senses, resolving apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first opinion
Carefully read the Arabic and translation for the first opinion that inability is opposite of ability. Note the analogy with will and aversion.
Notice how they argue that opposite attributes must share the same relatum.
2
Examine the second opinion and its proof
The second opinion relies on consensus that a trembling person is unable to stand even though standing is non-existent. Consider the supporting argument about the challenger of the Qur'an.
This shows that inability can relate to the non-existent.
3
Understand the response about homonymy
The response clarifies that 'inability' can mean two different things: mere lack of ability or a positive attribute causing inability. Analyze the trembling person example to see the distinction.
This resolves the apparent conflict.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this discussion affects our understanding of divine attributes like ability and will.
This groundwork is essential for the later question of predetermination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد التاسع : المقدور يتبع الإرادة على القول الأول فلا سبق ولا تعلق بالضدين كما عرفت ( معتمد القول الأول ) الذي الأصح ( أنه ) أي العجز (ضد القدرة ) في جهة التعلق (فمتعلقهما واحد وإلا لما تضادا في التعلق ) والقدرة متعلقة بالموجود كما مر فيكون العجز متعلقاً به أيضاً، ونظير ذلك الإرادة والكراهة فإنهما لما تضادتا كان متعلقهما واحداً إذ لو اختلف متعلقهما لم تتضادا (و) معتمد القول ( الثاني : ( هو (الإجماع) من العقلاء (على عجز الزمن عن القيام مع أنه معدوم قال المصنف : ( ولو قيل : ( في الاستدلال على القول الثاني : إن لم يتعلق العجز بالمعدوم ) يلزم عدم عجز المتحدي بمعارضة القرآن ) أي يلزم أن لا يكون المتحدي بمعارضته عاجزاً عن الإتيان بمثله، بل يكون عاجزاً عن عدم الإتيان بمثله ( وأنه خلاف الإجماع ) لأن الأمة مجمعون على عجزه عن الإتيان بمثل القرآن ( و) خلاف (المعقول ) أيضاً لأن العقل يحكم أن المعارضة، إنما تكون بالأمثال لا بإعدامها ( لكان حسناً جداً (ويمكن الجواب ) عن الاستدلالين ( بأن العجز يقال باشتراك اللفظ لعدم القدرة ) وهو ظاهر ( ولصفة ) وجودية (تستعقب الفعل لا عن (قدرة كما في المرتعش فالزمن عاجز عن القيام بالمعنى الأول دون الثاني، وعاجز عن القعود بالمعني الثاني والمتحدون عاجزون بالمعنى الأول عن الإتيان بمثل القرآن وكأن الشيخ بني قوليه على هذين المعنيين كما أشير إليه . [ المقصد التاسع : المقدور يتبع الإرادة ] المقدور هل هو تبع للعلم أو للإرادة ؟ للمعتزلة فيه خلاف فمن قال منهم : هو (تبع للإرادة (فلأنه ) أي كون المقدور تبعاً للإرادة ) حقيقة القدرة ومقتضاها، فإنها صفة تؤثر على وفق الإرادة فيكون المقدور تبعاً للإرادة قطعاً (ومن قال ) منهم : هو تبع للعلم فلان صاحب الملكة في صناعة زاولها مدة مديدة ( يصدر عنها أفعال ) محكمة متقنة ) لا يقصدها أي لا يقصد تفاصيل أجزائها ولو قصدها لم توجد على تلك الوجوه من الحسن والإحكام ) فإن الكاتب ( الحاذق ( يراعي دقائق ) كثيرة ) في حرف واحد ) بلا قصد إليها ( ولو لاحظها وقصد إليها ( لفاته كثير منها وأما الأشاعرة فقد حكموا بأن مقدورات العباد مخلوقة لله تعالى بإرادته المتعلقة بتفاصيلها .
The Fourth Type - The Ninth Goal: The predetermined follows the will. According to the first opinion, there is neither priority nor relation to opposites, as you have learned. (The support of the first opinion) is that which is correct: (that it) i.e., inability (is opposite of ability) in the aspect of relation (so their relata are one, otherwise they would not be opposites in relation). And ability is related to the existent as has passed, so inability is related to it as well. An analogy is will and aversion, for when they are opposites, their relatum is one, because if their relata differed, they would not be opposites. (And) the support of the (second) opinion: (it is) (consensus) of rational people (on the inability of the trembling person to stand while it is non-existent). The author said: (And if it were said: (in the argument for the second opinion: If inability does not relate to the non-existent) then it would entail the challenger not being unable to produce the like of the Qur'an) i.e., it would entail that the challenger is not unable to produce its like, but rather is unable not to produce its like. (And that is contrary to consensus) because the community is unanimous on his inability to produce the like of the Qur'an, and (contrary to) reason as well because reason judges that challenge is only by likes, not by their non-existence. (It would be very good.) (And a response can be given) to both arguments (that inability is used by homonymy to mean lack of ability) which is apparent, (and to a positive attribute that necessitates an action not from ability) as in the trembling person. So the trembling person is unable to stand in the first sense but not the second, and able to sit in the second sense. And the challengers are unable in the first sense to produce the like of the Qur'an. It is as if the Shaykh built his two opinions on these two meanings, as indicated. [The Ninth Goal: The predetermined follows the will] Is the predetermined a follower of knowledge or will? The Mu'tazila have a disagreement on it. Whoever among them says: it is (a follower of will) (because) i.e., that the predetermined is a follower of will (is the reality of ability and its requirement, for it) is an attribute that acts in accordance with will, so the predetermined is definitely a follower of will. (And whoever) among them says: it is a follower of knowledge, because the possessor of a skill in a craft he has practiced for a long time (produces from it actions) precise and perfect (that he does not intend), i.e., he does not intend the details of their parts; and if he intended them, they would not occur in those aspects of goodness and perfection. For the (skilled) writer (observes) many (subtleties) in a single letter (without intending them), and (if he reflected on them and intended them) many of them would escape him. As for the Ash'aris, they ruled that the actions of servants are created by Allah Most High by His will related to their details.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents two closely related discussions under the Ninth Goal. First, it examines the nature of inability (al-ʿajz) and whether it is the opposite of ability (al-qudra). The author presents two opinions: the first holds that inability is indeed the opposite of ability, so they share the same relatum—if ability relates to an existent, inability relates to that same existent as non-existent. The second opinion relies on consensus: people agree that a trembling person is unable to stand even though standing is non-existent, proving that inability can relate to the non-existent. A supporting argument is given: if inability did not relate to the non-existent, the challenger of the Qur'an would not be unable to produce its like, which contradicts consensus and reason. The response is that 'inability' is used homonymously: one meaning is simple lack of ability, another is a positive attribute that necessitates an action not from ability. The trembling person is unable to stand in the first sense but not the second. The section then transitions to the main question: does the predetermined (al-maqdūr) follow knowledge (al-ʿilm) or will (al-irāda)? The Mu'tazila differ: some say it follows will because ability is an attribute that acts according to will. Others say it follows knowledge, giving the example of a skilled craftsman who produces precise actions without intending their details—if he intended them, they would not be perfect. The Ash'aris hold that all servants' actions are created directly by Allah's will, which encompasses every detail.

Key Terms
القدرة
العجز
ضد
تعلق
معدوم
موجود
اتفاق
ملكة
فعل محكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inability is always a simple lack of power.
Correction: Inability can also be a positive attribute that prevents action, as in the trembling person.

Checkpoint
What is the main support for the second opinion that inability relates to the non-existent?
A) Logical deduction from analogy with will and aversion.
B) Consensus that a trembling person is unable to stand even though standing is non-existent.
C) The argument that if inability only relates to existents, the Qur'anic challenger would not be unable.
D) The homonymous nature of the word 'inability'.

Key Takeaway
Inability (al-ʿajz) can mean either a simple lack of ability or a positive attribute that prevents action, and this distinction resolves disagreements over its relation to ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to perform a good deed or avoid a sin, do you attribute it to a simple lack of power or to a deeper, positive attribute within you that blocks action? Which understanding makes you more accountable to change?
?
The lesson shows that 'inability' can be a positive attribute that prevents action, not just an absence. Are there habits or attachments in your life that function as such a positive inability? What would it cost you to uproot them?
?
The challengers were unable to produce a like Qur'an due to lack of ability. If you claim inability to fulfill a command of Allah, are you sure it is a true lack of ability and not a positive attribute of negligence or desire? How does this distinction affect your responsibility before Him?

Does the Predetermined Follow Will or Knowledge?
Presents the Mu'tazila disagreement on whether al-maqdur follows al-ʿilm or al-irāda, with the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila dispute on whether the predetermined (al-maqdur) follows will (al-irāda) or knowledge (al-ʿilm), and the Ash'ari position that all actions are created by Allah's will.

Core Idea
The skilled craftsman's precise actions show that actions can follow knowledge even without intending details.

This lesson addresses a key debate within the Mu'tazila: Does the predetermined (al-maqdur) depend on Allah's will or His knowledge? We will see two views: one says it follows will because ability acts according to will; the other says it follows knowledge, giving the example of a skilled writer who produces perfect letters without intending their details. The Ash'ari position is then presented as settling the matter by asserting that all actions are created directly by Allah's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Mu'tazila debate
The passage starts with the question: Does the predetermined follow will or knowledge? Read the two opinions and their rationales carefully.
Note the distinction: will-based vs knowledge-based.
2
Analyze the example of the skilled craftsman
The knowledge-based view uses the example of a skilled writer who produces perfect letters without intending the subtleties. This shows actions can follow knowledge without explicit intention of details.
Consider why this supports the idea that actions follow knowledge.
3
Understand the Ash'ari position
The passage concludes: 'As for the Ash'aris, they ruled that the actions of servants are created by Allah Most High by His will related to their details.'
This is a distinct position beyond the Mu'tazila dispute.
4
Reflect on the implications for divine attributes
Think about how this debate relates to divine will and knowledge. Which attribute is primary in determining events?
This has consequences for understanding predestination.

النوع الرابع - المقصد التاسع : المقدور يتبع الإرادة على القول الأول فلا سبق ولا تعلق بالضدين كما عرفت ( معتمد القول الأول ) الذي الأصح ( أنه ) أي العجز (ضد القدرة ) في جهة التعلق (فمتعلقهما واحد وإلا لما تضادا في التعلق ) والقدرة متعلقة بالموجود كما مر فيكون العجز متعلقاً به أيضاً، ونظير ذلك الإرادة والكراهة فإنهما لما تضادتا كان متعلقهما واحداً إذ لو اختلف متعلقهما لم تتضادا (و) معتمد القول ( الثاني : ( هو (الإجماع) من العقلاء (على عجز الزمن عن القيام مع أنه معدوم قال المصنف : ( ولو قيل : ( في الاستدلال على القول الثاني : إن لم يتعلق العجز بالمعدوم ) يلزم عدم عجز المتحدي بمعارضة القرآن ) أي يلزم أن لا يكون المتحدي بمعارضته عاجزاً عن الإتيان بمثله، بل يكون عاجزاً عن عدم الإتيان بمثله ( وأنه خلاف الإجماع ) لأن الأمة مجمعون على عجزه عن الإتيان بمثل القرآن ( و) خلاف (المعقول ) أيضاً لأن العقل يحكم أن المعارضة، إنما تكون بالأمثال لا بإعدامها ( لكان حسناً جداً (ويمكن الجواب ) عن الاستدلالين ( بأن العجز يقال باشتراك اللفظ لعدم القدرة ) وهو ظاهر ( ولصفة ) وجودية (تستعقب الفعل لا عن (قدرة كما في المرتعش فالزمن عاجز عن القيام بالمعنى الأول دون الثاني، وعاجز عن القعود بالمعني الثاني والمتحدون عاجزون بالمعنى الأول عن الإتيان بمثل القرآن وكأن الشيخ بني قوليه على هذين المعنيين كما أشير إليه . [ المقصد التاسع : المقدور يتبع الإرادة ] المقدور هل هو تبع للعلم أو للإرادة ؟ للمعتزلة فيه خلاف فمن قال منهم : هو (تبع للإرادة (فلأنه ) أي كون المقدور تبعاً للإرادة ) حقيقة القدرة ومقتضاها، فإنها صفة تؤثر على وفق الإرادة فيكون المقدور تبعاً للإرادة قطعاً (ومن قال ) منهم : هو تبع للعلم فلان صاحب الملكة في صناعة زاولها مدة مديدة ( يصدر عنها أفعال ) محكمة متقنة ) لا يقصدها أي لا يقصد تفاصيل أجزائها ولو قصدها لم توجد على تلك الوجوه من الحسن والإحكام ) فإن الكاتب ( الحاذق ( يراعي دقائق ) كثيرة ) في حرف واحد ) بلا قصد إليها ( ولو لاحظها وقصد إليها ( لفاته كثير منها وأما الأشاعرة فقد حكموا بأن مقدورات العباد مخلوقة لله تعالى بإرادته المتعلقة بتفاصيلها .
The Fourth Type - The Ninth Goal: The predetermined follows the will. According to the first opinion, there is neither priority nor relation to opposites, as you have learned. (The support of the first opinion) is that which is correct: (that it) i.e., inability (is opposite of ability) in the aspect of relation (so their relata are one, otherwise they would not be opposites in relation). And ability is related to the existent as has passed, so inability is related to it as well. An analogy is will and aversion, for when they are opposites, their relatum is one, because if their relata differed, they would not be opposites. (And) the support of the (second) opinion: (it is) (consensus) of rational people (on the inability of the trembling person to stand while it is non-existent). The author said: (And if it were said: (in the argument for the second opinion: If inability does not relate to the non-existent) then it would entail the challenger not being unable to produce the like of the Qur'an) i.e., it would entail that the challenger is not unable to produce its like, but rather is unable not to produce its like. (And that is contrary to consensus) because the community is unanimous on his inability to produce the like of the Qur'an, and (contrary to) reason as well because reason judges that challenge is only by likes, not by their non-existence. (It would be very good.) (And a response can be given) to both arguments (that inability is used by homonymy to mean lack of ability) which is apparent, (and to a positive attribute that necessitates an action not from ability) as in the trembling person. So the trembling person is unable to stand in the first sense but not the second, and able to sit in the second sense. And the challengers are unable in the first sense to produce the like of the Qur'an. It is as if the Shaykh built his two opinions on these two meanings, as indicated. [The Ninth Goal: The predetermined follows the will] Is the predetermined a follower of knowledge or will? The Mu'tazila have a disagreement on it. Whoever among them says: it is (a follower of will) (because) i.e., that the predetermined is a follower of will (is the reality of ability and its requirement, for it) is an attribute that acts in accordance with will, so the predetermined is definitely a follower of will. (And whoever) among them says: it is a follower of knowledge, because the possessor of a skill in a craft he has practiced for a long time (produces from it actions) precise and perfect (that he does not intend), i.e., he does not intend the details of their parts; and if he intended them, they would not occur in those aspects of goodness and perfection. For the (skilled) writer (observes) many (subtleties) in a single letter (without intending them), and (if he reflected on them and intended them) many of them would escape him. As for the Ash'aris, they ruled that the actions of servants are created by Allah Most High by His will related to their details.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents two closely related discussions under the Ninth Goal. First, it examines the nature of inability (al-ʿajz) and whether it is the opposite of ability (al-qudra). The author presents two opinions: the first holds that inability is indeed the opposite of ability, so they share the same relatum—if ability relates to an existent, inability relates to that same existent as non-existent. The second opinion relies on consensus: people agree that a trembling person is unable to stand even though standing is non-existent, proving that inability can relate to the non-existent. A supporting argument is given: if inability did not relate to the non-existent, the challenger of the Qur'an would not be unable to produce its like, which contradicts consensus and reason. The response is that 'inability' is used homonymously: one meaning is simple lack of ability, another is a positive attribute that necessitates an action not from ability. The trembling person is unable to stand in the first sense but not the second. The section then transitions to the main question: does the predetermined (al-maqdūr) follow knowledge (al-ʿilm) or will (al-irāda)? The Mu'tazila differ: some say it follows will because ability is an attribute that acts according to will. Others say it follows knowledge, giving the example of a skilled craftsman who produces precise actions without intending their details—if he intended them, they would not be perfect. The Ash'aris hold that all servants' actions are created directly by Allah's will, which encompasses every detail.

Key Terms
المقدور
الإرادة
العلم
ملكة
فعل محكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position is the same as the Mu'tazila opinion that predetermined follows will.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is distinct: they assert that all actions are created by Allah's will, not merely that predetermined follows will. The Mu'tazila debate is about the basis of the predetermined's dependency, while Ash'aris affirm direct divine creation.

Checkpoint
What example is used to support the view that the predetermined follows knowledge?
A) A trembling person unable to stand.
B) A writer producing perfect letters without intending details.
C) The challenger of the Qur'an.
D) The relation of ability to existent.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila disagreed on whether predetermined follows will or knowledge, while Ash'aris assert all actions are created by Allah's will in their entirety.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform even the simplest action, do you assume that every detail is under your conscious control, or have you considered that Allah's knowledge encompasses subtleties you never intended? How does this affect your reliance on your own planning versus trust in Allah's knowledge?
?
If the Ash'aris believe that all your actions are created by Allah's will in their entirety, how should that change the way you approach repentance for sins you committed inadvertently? Will you today acknowledge that even your mistakes fall under His will and seek His forgiveness with humility?
?
The skilled writer analogy shows that perfect actions can flow from knowledge without intending every detail. In your own daily deeds, where have you seen divine knowledge guiding you beyond your own will? Will you thank Allah for those moments and ask Him to increase your reliance on His knowledge over your own limited planning?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
Pleasure and Pain: Self-Evident and Indefinable
The author states that pleasure and pain are self-evident, known by inner sensation, and not definable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pleasure and pain are considered self-evident and why they cannot be defined through acquisition.

Core Idea
Pleasure and pain are known directly from within, not through definitions.

The author introduces the fifth type of psychological qualities, focusing on pleasure and pain. He argues that these are not learned concepts but directly experienced by every sentient being. This passage establishes that they are self-evident and thus cannot be defined in the usual way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms and try to grasp the author's main claim before proceeding.
2
Understand self-evidence
The author states that every rational or sentient being perceives pleasure and pain from themselves, distinguishes them, and knows them necessarily. This is the basis for calling them 'self-evident'.
Reflect on your own experience of pleasure and pain — do you need a definition to know them?
3
Recognize the argument against definition
Because their essences are known through direct inner sensation, any attempt to define them would be redundant. The author explains that definition aims to acquire knowledge, but here knowledge is already present.
4
Consider the alternative definition
The passage mentions a definition: 'Pleasure is the perception of the agreeable.' But this is only for verbal clarification, not to reveal the essence.
Think: why might someone offer a definition even if the thing is self-evident?
5
Reflect on the overall significance
This discussion highlights the limits of rational definition and affirms that direct experience is a valid source of knowledge.
Connect this to broader themes in Islamic theology about knowledge and experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس من أنواع الكيفيات النفسانية في بقية الكيفيات النفسانية وفيه مقصدان . المقصد الأول : اللذة والألم من بقية الكيفيات النفسانية اللذة والألم بديهيان لأن كل عاقل بل كل حساس يدركهما من نفسه، ويميز كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه، ويميزهما عما عداهما بالضرورة فلا يعرفان لتحصيل ماهيتهما، فإن الإحساس الوجداني بجزئياتهما قد أفاد العلم بتلك الماهية على وجه لا يتأتى لنا تحصيل مثله بطريق الاكتساب كما في سائر المحسوسات على ما مر، وهذا مما لا يخفى على ذي إنصاف، نعم قد يقصد في المحسوسات شرح الاسم وذكر الخواص دفعا للالتباس اللفظي وقيل : اللذة إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم قوله : ( بديهيان) أي بالكنه الإجمالي الحاصل من حصول جزئياتهما في الخيال وحذف مشخصاتها في سائر المحسوسات . قوله : (من نفسه أي وجداناً حاصلاً من نفسه لا من النظر. قوله على وجه لا يتأتى إلخ لأن هذه حصول لماهيتها بأنفسهما والحاصل بالتعريف حصولها بالوجه بناء على أن الاطلاع على ذاتيات الحقائق متعذر . قوله : (شرح الاسم ومآله التصديق بالوضع للالتباس اللفظي بين الأمور الحاصلة في الذهن لا تحصيل ما لم يكن حاصلاً . قوله : ( يدركهما من نفسه الأقرب أن من بمعنى في كما في قوله تعالى أروني ماذا خلقوا من الأرض﴾ [فاطر:٤٠] .
The Fifth Type of the kinds of psychological qualities: on the remaining psychological qualities, and it contains two aims. The First Aim: Pleasure and pain are among the remaining psychological qualities. Pleasure and pain are self-evident, because every rational being, indeed every sentient being, perceives them from himself, distinguishes each one from the other, and distinguishes them from what is other than them necessarily; thus they are not known for the acquisition of their essence, for the inner sensation of their particulars has provided knowledge of that essence in a way that it is not possible for us to acquire its like by way of acquisition, as in the case of other sensible things as previously mentioned. And this is not hidden from anyone fair-minded. Indeed, in the case of sensible things, one may intend explanation of the name and mention of properties to remove verbal confusion. And it was said: Pleasure is the perception of the agreeable insofar as it is agreeable. His saying: (They are self-evident) i.e., by the summary core that results from the occurrence of their particulars in the imagination and the removal of their specifics in other sensible things. His saying: (from himself) i.e., as an inner experience occurring from himself, not from reflection. His saying: (in a way that it is not possible) etc., because this is an occurrence of their essences by themselves, and what is obtained by definition is an occurrence of it by way [of essence], based on the fact that insight into the essential properties of realities is unattainable. His saying: (explanation of the name and its outcome is assent to the convention for verbal confusion between matters occurring in the mind, not acquisition of what was not present). His saying: (perceives them from himself) — the closest is that 'from' is in the sense of 'in', as in His saying Almighty: 'Show Me what they have created in the earth' [Fatir:40].
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth type of psychological qualities, with two aims. The first aim concerns pleasure (al-ladhdha) and pain (al-alam). The author states that they are self-evident (badīhiyyān) because every rational or sentient being perceives them from within themselves and distinguishes them necessarily. Therefore, they are not defined to acquire their essence, since inner sensation of their particulars gives knowledge of that essence in a way that cannot be acquired through learning, like other sensibles. However, one may explain the name or mention properties to remove verbal confusion. An alternative definition is mentioned: pleasure is the perception of the agreeable insofar as it is agreeable. The commentary explains that 'self-evident' means by the summary core obtained from particulars in the imagination; 'from himself' means inner experience not from reflection; the impossibility of definition is because essences are known directly; explanation of names is for verbal clarification; and the phrase 'perceives them from himself' takes 'from' as 'in', citing Quran 35:40.

Key Terms
اللذة
الألم
بديهيان
الإحساس الوجداني
شرح الاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure and pain can be defined and their essences acquired through learning.
Correction: The author argues that pleasure and pain are self-evident and known directly by inner sensation, so definition cannot add to that knowledge. Definitions only serve to clarify terms verbally.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that pleasure and pain are not defined (لا يعرفان)?
A) Because they are too complex to define
B) Because their essences are known directly through inner sensation, not through acquisition
C) Because they are not real qualities
D) Because they are only known through rational proof

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and pain are self-evident, known directly through inner sensation, and cannot be defined because their essence is already present in experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you truly felt pleasure or pain without trying to define or rationalize it? Did you need anyone to tell you it was real?
?
If pleasure and pain are self-evident to every sentient being, why do we sometimes ignore our own inner sensations in favor of what others define as 'good' or 'bad'? Will you commit to trusting your own direct experience of what your heart finds agreeable or painful, even when external definitions disagree?
?
The author says definitions only remove verbal confusion, not provide real knowledge. How often do you rely on definitions from scholars or teachers to understand your own spiritual states? Are you willing to sit with the raw sensation of your own soul without needing a label for it?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

Pleasure as Perception of the Agreeable
Defines pleasure and explains the agreeable with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core definition of pleasure as the soul's perception of what is agreeable, not the agreeable object itself.

Core Idea
Pleasure is not the object; it is the soul's awareness of a suitable perfection.

The commentator begins by defining pleasure as a type of psychic quality. He presents the key term 'agreeable' (mula'im) and illustrates it with examples from different faculties. This passage establishes that pleasure is an inner perception, not an external thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first few lines carefully to find the definition of pleasure. Note the phrase 'insofar as it is agreeable'.
2
Identify the examples
List the examples of pleasure given for different faculties: taste, hearing, irascible, rational. Notice the pattern: each is a perception of something suitable.
3
Understand 'agreeable'
The commentator explains that 'agreeable' means perfection that is suitable. Distinguish between absolute perfection and perfection restricted by suitability.
The restricted sense is meant here.
4
Clarify perception vs. object
Note the discussion about imagining a beautiful form: pleasure is the perception of the perception, not the object itself. The soul observes the perception.
